   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">

   </h1>

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      This article is about criticism of the doctrines and practices of Christianity.  For negative attitudes towards Christians, see
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Christian_sentiment" title="Anti-Christian sentiment">
       Anti-Christian sentiment
      </a>
      .

       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:Criticism_of_religion_sidebar" title="Template:Criticism of religion sidebar">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Criticism_of_religion_sidebar" title="Template talk:Criticism of religion sidebar">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Criticism_of_religion_sidebar&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      <b>
       Criticism of Christianity
      </b>
      varies from the criticism of Christian beliefs, teachings, history, activities, and
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_terrorism" title="Christian terrorism">
       terrorism
      </a>
      . Throughout the
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">
       history of Christianity
      </a>
      , many have criticized
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_Church" title="Christian Church">
       church
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_Jesus" title="Criticism of Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bible#Christian_Bibles" title="Bible">
       Christian Bible
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christians
      </a>
      and other elements of Christianity.
     </p>
     <p>
      The formal response of Christians to such criticisms is described as
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">
       Christian apologetics
      </a>
      . Several areas of criticism also include the claims of scripture itself, the ethics of biblical interpretations that have been used historically to justify certain attitudes and behaviors, the question of the religion's compatibility with science, and other Christian doctrines. The criticism came from the various religious and non-religious groups around the world, some of whom were themselves Christians.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Scripture">
       Scripture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Scripture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Criticism_of_the_Bible" title="Criticism of the Bible">
       Criticism of the Bible
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Biblical_criticism">
       Biblical criticism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Biblical criticism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">
       Biblical criticism
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Bible_and_History" title="The Bible and History">
       The Bible and History
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Internal_consistency_and_the_Bible" title="Internal consistency and the Bible">
       Internal consistency and the Bible
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Biblical criticism, in particular higher criticism, covers a variety of methods used since the
      <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">
       Enlightenment
      </a>
      in the early 18th century as scholars began to apply to biblical documents the same methods and perspectives which had already been applied to other literary and philosophical texts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is an umbrella term covering various techniques used mainly by mainline and liberal Christian theologians to study the meaning of biblical passages. It uses general historical principles, and is based primarily on reason rather than revelation or faith. There are four primary types of biblical criticism:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RobinsonCriticism_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RobinsonCriticism-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Form criticism:
       </i>
       an analysis of literary documents, particularly the Bible, to discover earlier oral traditions (stories, legends, myths, etc.) upon which they were based.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Tradition criticism:
       </i>
       an analysis of the Bible, concentrating on how religious traditions grew and changed over the time span during which the text was written.
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Higher criticism:
       </i>
       the study of the sources and literary methods employed by the biblical authors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mather_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mather-3">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         3
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RobinsonCriticism_2-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-RobinsonCriticism-2">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         2
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Lower criticism:
       </i>
       the discipline and study of the actual wording of the Bible; a quest for textual purity and understanding.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mather_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mather-3">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         3
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Inconsistencies have been pointed out by critics and skeptics,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      presenting as difficulties the different numbers and names for the same feature and different sequences for what is supposed to be the same event. Responses to these criticisms include the modern
      <a href="/wiki/Documentary_hypothesis" title="Documentary hypothesis">
       documentary hypothesis
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Two_source_hypothesis" title="Two source hypothesis">
       two source hypothesis
      </a>
      (in various guises), and assertions that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pastoral_Epistles" title="Pastoral Epistles">
       Pastoral Epistles
      </a>
      are pseudonymous. Contrasting with these critical stances are positions supported by literalists, considering the texts to be consistent, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Torah" title="Torah">
       Torah
      </a>
      written by a single source,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phelan_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Phelan-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the Gospels by four independent witnesses,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-France2_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-France2-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and all of the Pauline Epistles, except possibly the
      <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_the_Hebrews" title="Epistle to the Hebrews">
       Hebrews
      </a>
      , as having been written by
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_the_Apostle" title="Paul the Apostle">
       Paul the Apostle
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      While consideration of the context is necessary when studying the Bible, some find the accounts of the
      <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_Jesus" title="Resurrection of Jesus">
       Resurrection of Jesus
      </a>
      within the four Gospels of Matthew, Mark, Luke and John, difficult to reconcile.
      <a href="/wiki/E._P._Sanders" title="E. P. Sanders">
       E. P. Sanders
      </a>
      concludes that the inconsistencies make the possibility of a deliberate fraud unlikely: "A plot to foster belief in the Resurrection would probably have resulted in a more consistent story. Instead, there seems to have been a competition: 'I saw him,' 'So did I,' 'The women saw him first,' 'No, I did; they didn't see him at all,' and so on."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Harold_Lindsell" title="Harold Lindsell">
       Harold Lindsell
      </a>
      points out that it is a "gross distortion" to state that people who believe in
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">
       biblical inerrancy
      </a>
      suppose every statement made in the Bible is true (opposed to accurate).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindsell_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lindsell-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He indicates there are expressly false statements in the Bible which are reported accurately
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindsell_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lindsell-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (for example, Satan is a liar whose lies are accurately reported as to what he actually said).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindsell_9-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lindsell-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Proponents of biblical inerrancy generally do not teach that the Bible was dictated directly by God, but that God used the "distinctive personalities and literary styles of the writers" of scripture and that
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">
       God's inspiration
      </a>
      guided them to flawlessly project his message through their own language and personality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chicago_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chicago-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :Art. VIII
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Those who believe in the
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inspiration" title="Biblical inspiration">
       inspiration
      </a>
      of scripture teach that it is
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_infallibility" title="Biblical infallibility">
       infallible
      </a>
      (or inerrant), that is, free from error in the truths it expresses by its character as the word of God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the scope of what this encompasses is disputed, as the term includes 'faith and practice' positions, with some denominations holding that the historical or scientific details, which may be irrelevant to matters of faith and Christian practice, may contain errors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inerrancy_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-inerrancy-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other scholars take stronger views,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but for a few verses these positions require more exegetical work, leading to dispute (compare the serious debate over the related issue of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Clarity_of_scripture" title="Clarity of scripture">
        perspicuity
       </a>
      </i>
      , attracting biblical and philosophical discussion).
     </p>
     <p>
      Infallibility refers to the original texts of the Bible, and all mainstream scholars acknowledge the potential for human error in transmission and translation; yet, through use
      <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">
       textual criticism
      </a>
      modern (critical) copies are considered to "faithfully represent the original",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chicago_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chicago-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :Art. X
      </sup>
      and our understanding of the original language sufficiently well for accurate translation. The opposing view is that there is too much corruption, or translation too difficult, to agree with modern texts.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Judaic_view:_Unfulfilled_prophecy">
       Judaic view: Unfulfilled prophecy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Judaic view: Unfulfilled prophecy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abrahamthreeangels.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="797" data-file-width="541" height="324" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Abrahamthreeangels.jpg/220px-Abrahamthreeangels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Abrahamthreeangels.jpg/330px-Abrahamthreeangels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Abrahamthreeangels.jpg/440px-Abrahamthreeangels.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abrahamthreeangels.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        God reveals himself to
        <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">
         Abraham
        </a>
        in scripture and he is seen here with three angels. By
        <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Tiepolo" title="Giovanni Battista Tiepolo">
         Giovanni Battista Tiepolo
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Hundreds of years before the time of Jesus, Jewish prophets promised that a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Messiah" title="Jewish Messiah">
       messiah
      </a>
      would come.
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Judaism
      </a>
      claims that Jesus did not fulfill these prophecies. Other skeptics usually claim that the prophecies are either vague or unfulfilled,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or that the Old Testament writings influenced the composition of New Testament narratives.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">
       Christian apologists
      </a>
      claim that Jesus fulfilled these
      <a href="/wiki/Bible_prophecy" title="Bible prophecy">
       prophecies
      </a>
      , which they argue are nearly impossible to fulfill by chance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many Christians anticipate the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">
       Second Coming
      </a>
      of Jesus, when he will fulfill the rest of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Messianic_prophecy" title="Messianic prophecy">
       Messianic prophecy
      </a>
      , such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Last_Judgment" title="Last Judgment">
       Last Judgment
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Resurrection_of_the_dead" title="Resurrection of the dead">
       general resurrection
      </a>
      , establishment of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_God" title="Kingdom of God">
       Kingdom of God
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Messianic_Age" title="Messianic Age">
       Messianic Age
      </a>
      (see the article on
      <a href="/wiki/Preterism" title="Preterism">
       Preterism
      </a>
      for contrasting Christian views).
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      traces Jesus' line to that of David; however, according to
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_L._Harris" title="Stephen L. Harris">
       Stephen L. Harris
      </a>
      :
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       Jesus did not accomplish what Israel's prophets said the Messiah was commissioned to do: He did not deliver the covenant people from their Gentile enemies, reassemble those scattered in the Diaspora, restore the Davidic kingdom, or establish universal peace (cf.
       <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Isaiah.9%3A6-7%3B11%3A7-12%3A16&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">
         Isa. 9:67; 11:712:16
        </a>
       </span>
       , etc.). Instead of freeing Jews from oppressors and thereby fulfilling God's ancient promisesfor land, nationhood, kingship, and blessingJesus died a "shameful" death, defeated by the very political powers the Messiah was prophesied to overcome. Indeed, the Hebrew prophets did not foresee that Israel's savior would be executed as a common criminal by Gentiles, making Jesus' crucifixion a "stumbling block" to scripturally literate Jews. (
       <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Cor+1%3A23&amp;version=niv" rel="nofollow">
         1 Cor.1:23
        </a>
       </span>
       )
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Christian preachers counter this argument by stating that these prophecies will be fulfilled by Jesus in the
      <a href="/wiki/Millennialism" title="Millennialism">
       Millennial Reign
      </a>
      after the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Tribulation" title="Great Tribulation">
       Great Tribulation
      </a>
      , according to
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      prophecies, especially in the
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">
       Book of Revelation
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The 16th-century
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Jewish
      </a>
      theologian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isaac_b._Abraham_of_Troki" title="Isaac b. Abraham of Troki">
       Isaac ben Abraham
      </a>
      , who lived in
      <a href="/wiki/Trakai" title="Trakai">
       Trakai, Lithuania
      </a>
      , penned a work called
      <i>
       Chizzuk Emunah
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       Faith Strengthened
      </i>
      ) that attempted to refute the ideas that
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      was the
      <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">
       Messiah
      </a>
      prophesied in the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old Testament
      </a>
      and that Christianity was the "
      <a href="/wiki/New_Covenant" title="New Covenant">
       New Covenant
      </a>
      " of
      <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
       God
      </a>
      . He systematically identified a number of inconsistencies in the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      , contradictions between the New Testament and the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old Testament
      </a>
      , and Old Testament prophesies which remained unfulfilled in Jesus' lifetime. In addition, he questioned a number of Christian practices, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunday_Sabbath" title="Sunday Sabbath">
       Sunday Sabbath
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trokibio_18-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-trokibio-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Written originally for
      <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">
       Jews
      </a>
      to persuade them not to convert to
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the work was eventually read by Christians. While the well-known Christian Hebraist
      <a href="/wiki/Johann_Christoph_Wagenseil" title="Johann Christoph Wagenseil">
       Johann Christoph Wagenseil
      </a>
      attempted an elaborate refutation of Abraham's arguments, Wagenseil's
      <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
       Latin
      </a>
      translation of it only increased interest in the work and inspired later Christian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freethinkers" title="Freethinkers">
       freethinkers
      </a>
      .
      <i>
       Chizzuk Emunah
      </i>
      was praised as a masterpiece by
      <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">
       Voltaire
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trokibio_18-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-trokibio-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On the other hand,
      <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">
       Blaise Pascal
      </a>
      believed that "[t]he prophecies are the strongest proof of Jesus Christ". He wrote that Jesus was foretold, and that the prophecies came from a succession of people over a span of four thousand years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Apologist
      <a href="/wiki/Josh_McDowell" title="Josh McDowell">
       Josh McDowell
      </a>
      defends the fulfillment of Old Testament prophecy as supporting Christianity, arguing that prophecies fulfilled by Christ include ones relating to his ancestral line, birthplace, virgin birth, miracles, manner of death, and resurrection. He says that even the timing of the
      <a href="/wiki/Messiah" title="Messiah">
       Messiah
      </a>
      in years and in relation to events is predicted, and that the Jewish
      <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">
       Talmud
      </a>
      (not accepting Jesus as the Messiah, see also
      <a href="/wiki/Rejection_of_Jesus" title="Rejection of Jesus">
       Rejection of Jesus
      </a>
      ) laments that the Messiah had not appeared despite the scepter being taken away from Judah.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Selective_interpretation">
       Selective interpretation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Selective interpretation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Expounding_of_the_Law" title="Expounding of the Law">
       Expounding of the Law
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biblical_law_in_Christianity" title="Biblical law in Christianity">
       Biblical law in Christianity
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cafeteria_Christianity" title="Cafeteria Christianity">
       Cafeteria Christianity
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Critics argue that the selective invocation of portions of the Old Testament is hypocritical, particularly when those portions endorse hostility towards women and homosexuals, when other portions are considered obsolete. The entire Mosaic Law is described in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Galatians+3%3A24-25&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        Galatians 3:24-25
       </a>
      </span>
      as a tutor which is no longer necessary, according to some interpretations, see also
      <a href="/wiki/Antinomianism#Antinomianism_in_the_New_Testament" title="Antinomianism">
       Antinomianism in the New Testament
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      On the other hand, many of the
      <a href="/wiki/Abrogation_of_Old_Covenant_laws" title="Abrogation of Old Covenant laws">
       Old Testament laws are seen as specifically abrogated
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      , such as
      <a href="/wiki/Circumcision_controversy_in_early_Christianity" title="Circumcision controversy in early Christianity">
       circumcision
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though this may simply be a parallel to Jewish
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Noahide_Laws" title="Noahide Laws">
       Noahide Laws
      </a>
      . See also
      <a href="/wiki/Split_of_early_Christianity_and_Judaism" title="Split of early Christianity and Judaism">
       Split of early Christianity and Judaism
      </a>
      . On the other hand, other passages are pro-Law, such as Romans 3:31: "Do we then make void the law through faith? Certainly not! On the contrary, we establish the law." See also
      <a href="/wiki/Antinomianism#Pauline_passages_opposing_antinomianism" title="Antinomianism">
       Pauline passages opposing antinomianism
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      There are a number of positions which are taken in response to these critics:
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Some argue that the specific principles invoked by Christians are endorsed or renewed in the New Testament.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
        <a href="#cite_note-23">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         23
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       Others argue that the Old Testament law applies, except as modified by the New Testament.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         24
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_corruption">
       Textual corruption
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Textual corruption">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_criticism" title="Biblical criticism">
       Biblical criticism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">
       Textual criticism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Within the abundance of biblical manuscripts exist a number of textual variants. The vast majority of these textual variants are the inconsequential misspelling of words, word order variations
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the mistranscription of abbreviations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Text critics such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bart_D._Ehrman" title="Bart D. Ehrman">
       Bart D. Ehrman
      </a>
      have proposed that some of these textual variants and interpolations were theologically motivated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Ehrman's conclusions and textual variant choices have been challenged by reviewers, including
      <a href="/wiki/Daniel_B._Wallace" title="Daniel B. Wallace">
       Daniel B. Wallace
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Craig_Blomberg" title="Craig Blomberg">
       Craig Blomberg
      </a>
      and Thomas Howe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In attempting to determine the original text of the New Testament books, some modern textual critics have identified sections as probably not original. In modern translations of the Bible, the results of textual criticism have led to certain verses being left out or marked as not original. These possible later additions include the following:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Metzger1994_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Metzger1994-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       The ending of
       <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mark" title="Gospel of Mark">
        Mark
       </a>
       <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mk.+16&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
         [Mk. 16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       The story in
       <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_John" title="Gospel of John">
        John
       </a>
       of the woman taken in adultery, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pericope_Adulterae" title="Pericope Adulterae">
        Pericope Adulterae
       </a>
      </li>
      <li>
       An explicit reference to the
       <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">
        Trinity
       </a>
       in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1_John" title="1 John">
        1 John
       </a>
       , the
       <a href="/wiki/Comma_Johanneum" title="Comma Johanneum">
        Comma Johanneum
       </a>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Most Bibles have footnotes to indicate areas which have disputed source documents. Bible Commentaries also discuss these, sometimes in great detail.
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <i>
       The Text Of The New Testament
      </i>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kurt_Aland" title="Kurt Aland">
       Kurt
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Aland" title="Barbara Aland">
       Barbara Aland
      </a>
      compare the total number of variant-free verses, and the number of variants per page (excluding
      <a href="/wiki/Orthography" title="Orthography">
       orthographic
      </a>
      errors), among the seven major editions of the Greek NT (
      <a href="/wiki/Constantin_von_Tischendorf#Works" title="Constantin von Tischendorf">
       Tischendorf
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_New_Testament_in_the_Original_Greek" title="The New Testament in the Original Greek">
       Westcott-Hort
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Hermann,_Freiherr_von_Soden" title="Hermann, Freiherr von Soden">
       von Soden
      </a>
      , Vogels, Merk, Bover and
      <a href="/wiki/Novum_Testamentum_Graece" title="Novum Testamentum Graece">
       Nestle-Aland
      </a>
      ) concluding 62.9%, or 4999/7947, agreement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aland_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Aland-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They concluded, "Thus in nearly two-thirds of the New Testament text, the seven editions of the Greek New Testament which we have reviewed are in complete accord, with no differences other than in orthographical details (e.g., the spelling of names, etc.). Verses in which any one of the seven editions differs by a single word are not counted. This result is quite amazing, demonstrating a far greater agreement among the Greek texts of the New Testament during the past century than
      <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">
       textual scholars
      </a>
      would have suspected In the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gospels" title="Gospels">
       Gospels
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_the_Apostles" title="Acts of the Apostles">
       Acts
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Revelation" title="Book of Revelation">
       Revelation
      </a>
      the agreement is less, while in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epistles" title="Epistles">
       letters
      </a>
      it is much greater."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aland_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Aland-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With the discovery of the Hebrew Bible texts among the
      <a href="/wiki/Dead_Sea_Scrolls" title="Dead Sea Scrolls">
       Dead Sea Scrolls
      </a>
      , questions have been raised about the textual accuracy of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Masoretic_text" title="Masoretic text">
       Masoretic text
      </a>
      . That is, whether the Masoretic text which forms the basis of most modern English translations of the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old Testament
      </a>
      , or translations which pre-date the masoretic text, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">
       Septuagint
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syriac_Peshitta" title="Syriac Peshitta">
       Syriac Peshitta
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Samaritan_Pentateuch" title="Samaritan Pentateuch">
       Samaritan Pentateuch
      </a>
      are more accurate.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mistranslation">
       Mistranslation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Mistranslation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Bible_errata" title="Bible errata">
       Bible errata
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations" title="Bible translations">
       Bible translations
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_translations_of_the_Bible" title="English translations of the Bible">
       English translations of the Bible
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Translation has given rise to a number of issues, as the original languages are often quite different in grammar as well as word meaning. While the
      <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Statement_on_Biblical_Inerrancy" title="Chicago Statement on Biblical Inerrancy">
       Chicago Statement on Biblical Inerrancy
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chicago_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chicago-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      states that
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_inerrancy" title="Biblical inerrancy">
       inerrancy
      </a>
      applies only to the original
      <a href="/wiki/Language" title="Language">
       languages
      </a>
      , some believers trust their own translation to be the accurate one. One such group of believers is known as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King-James-Only_Movement" title="King-James-Only Movement">
       King-James-Only Movement
      </a>
      . For readability, clarity, or other reasons, translators may choose different wording or sentence structure, and some translations may choose to paraphrase passages. Because some of the words in the original language have ambiguous or difficult to translate meanings, debates over the correct interpretation occur.
     </p>
     <p>
      Criticisms are also sometimes raised because of inconsistencies arising between different English translations of the Hebrew or Greek text. Some Christian interpretations are criticized for reflecting specific doctrinal bias
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jfj_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-jfj-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or a variant reading between the Masoretic Hebrew and Septuagint Greek manuscripts often quoted in the New Testament.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Translation_of_Almah_as_Virgin">
       Translation of
       <i>
        Almah
       </i>
       as
       <i>
        Virgin
       </i>
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Translation of Almah as Virgin">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+1%3A22-1%3A23&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">
        Matthew 1:22-1:23
       </a>
      </span>
      reads: "Now all this was done, that it might be fulfilled which was spoken of the Lord by the prophet, saying,
      <small>
       23
      </small>
      Behold, a virgin shall be with child, and shall bring forth a son, and they shall call his name Emmanuel, which being interpreted is, God with us." As early as the 2nd century CE, Jewish critics have argued that Christians were mistaken in their reading of the word
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Almah" title="Almah">
        almah
       </a>
      </i>
      ("") in
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Isaiah&amp;verse=7:14&amp;src=HE">
       Isaiah 7:14
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jewish translations of the verse from Isaiah read: "Behold, the
      <i>
       young woman
      </i>
      is with child and will bear a son and
      <i>
       she
      </i>
      will call his name Immanuel." Moreover, it is claimed that Christians have taken this verse out of context (see
      <a href="/wiki/Immanuel" title="Immanuel">
       Immanuel
      </a>
      for further information).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jfj_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-jfj-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Christians claim that the reference to the
      <i>
       seed of the woman
      </i>
      in Genesis 3:15 refers to a virgin birth, but critics claim otherwise.
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       And I will put enmity between thee and
       <u>
        the woman
       </u>
       , and between thy seed and
       <u>
        her seed
       </u>
       ; it shall bruise thy head, and thou shalt bruise his heel. (
       <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Genesis+3%3A15&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">
         Genesis 3:15
        </a>
       </span>
       )
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The Greek text of
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+1%3A23&amp;version=KJV%3BTR1550" rel="nofollow">
        Matthew 1:23
       </a>
      </span>
      uses the term "parthenos", which is the usual Greek word for virgin:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       " 
       <i>
        
       </i>
             
       <i>
        
       </i>
              '   ". (
       <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
        <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+1%3A23&amp;version=TR1550" rel="nofollow">
         Matthew 1:23
        </a>
       </span>
       <a href="/wiki/Textus_Receptus" title="Textus Receptus">
        Textus Receptus
       </a>
       )
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
        <a href="#cite_note-34">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         34
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The (right-to-left) Hebrew of
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Isaiah+7%3A14&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        Isaiah 7:14
       </a>
      </span>
      uses the word
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Almah" title="Almah">
        almah
       </a>
      </i>
      :
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <span style="font-size:140%">
        <span class="Unicode">
             , --: 
         <b>
          
         </b>
         ,   ,  ,  . 14
        </span>
       </span>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <dl>
        <dd>
         Therefore the Lord Himself shall give you a sign: behold,
         <u>
          the young woman
         </u>
         shall conceive, and bear a son, and shall call his name Immanuel. (
         <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Isaiah&amp;verse=7:14&amp;src=HE">
          Isaiah 7:14
         </a>
         )
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
          <a href="#cite_note-35">
           <span>
            [
           </span>
           35
           <span>
            ]
           </span>
          </a>
         </sup>
        </dd>
       </dl>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      The Jewish translation of the Hebrew scriptures into Greek that was in use during the 1st century, the
      <a href="/wiki/Septuagint" title="Septuagint">
       Septuagint
      </a>
      , uses the word "parthenos" ("virgin") in Isaiah 7:14 rather than the usual Greek word "neanis" for "young woman".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Septuagint's Greek term
      <i>
       <span lang="grc" xml:lang="grc">
        
       </span>
      </i>
      (parthenos) is considered by many to be an inexact rendering of the Hebrew word
      <i>
       `almah
      </i>
      in the text of Isaiah, but only in light of the Masoretic Canon which was finalized nearly 1000 years after the Septuagint.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODCC_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ODCC-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some scholars contend that debates over the precise meaning of
      <i>
       bethulah
      </i>
      (""-virgin) and
      <i>
       almah
      </i>
      (young woman) are misguided because no Hebrew word encapsulates the idea of certain virginity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther" title="Martin Luther">
       Martin Luther
      </a>
      also argued that the debate was irrelevant, not because the words do not clearly mean virgin, but because
      <i>
       almah
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       bethulah
      </i>
      were functional synonyms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      (For more information, see the articles on the
      <a href="/wiki/Virgin_birth_of_Jesus" title="Virgin birth of Jesus">
       Virgin birth of Jesus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Isaiah_7:14" title="Isaiah 7:14">
       Isaiah 7:14
      </a>
      .)
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Prophecy_of_the_Nazarene">
       Prophecy of the Nazarene
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Prophecy of the Nazarene">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Another example is
      <a href="/wiki/Nazarene_(title)" title="Nazarene (title)">
       Nazarene
      </a>
      in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+2%3A23&amp;version=9" rel="nofollow">
        Matthew 2:23
       </a>
      </span>
      : "And he came and dwelt in a city called Nazareth, that it might be fulfilled which was
      <u>
       spoken
      </u>
      by the prophets, He shall be called a Nazarene." The website for
      <a href="/wiki/Jews_for_Judaism" title="Jews for Judaism">
       Jews for Judaism
      </a>
      claims that "Since a Nazarene is a resident of the city of Nazareth and this city did not exist during the time period of the Jewish Bible, it is impossible to find this quotation in the Hebrew Scriptures. It was fabricated."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jfj_32-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-jfj-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, one common suggestion is that the New Testament verse is based on a passage relating to
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nazirite" title="Nazirite">
        Nazirites
       </a>
      </i>
      , either because this was a misunderstanding common at the time, or through deliberate re-reading of the term by the early Christians. Another suggestion is "that Matthew was playing on the similarity of the Hebrew word
      <i>
       nezer
      </i>
      (translated 'Branch' or 'shoot' in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Isaiah+11%3A1&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        Isaiah 11:1
       </a>
      </span>
      and
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jeremiah+23%3A5&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        Jeremiah 23:5
       </a>
      </span>
      ) with the Greek
      <i>
       nazoraios,
      </i>
      here translated 'Nazarene.'"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sper_1997_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sper_1997-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Christians also suggest that by using an indirect quotation and the plural term prophets, "Matthew was only saying that by living in Nazareth, Jesus was fulfilling the many Old Testament prophecies that He would be despised and rejected."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The background for this is illustrated by Philip's initial response in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+1%3A46&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        John 1:46
       </a>
      </span>
      to the idea that Jesus might be the Messiah: "Nazareth! Can anything good come from there?"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sper_1997_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sper_1997-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Miracles">
       Miracles
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Miracles">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">
       Miracle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">
       Faith healing
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Exorcism" title="Exorcism">
       Exorcism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Philosopher
      <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">
       David Hume
      </a>
      argued against the plausibility of miracles:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <div class="poem">
       <p>
        1) A miracle is a violation of the known laws of nature;
        <br/>
        2) We know these laws through repeated and constant experience;
        <br/>
        3) The testimony of those who report miracles contradicts the operation of known scientific laws;
        <br/>
        4) Consequently no one can rationally believe in miracles.
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Hume's argument against the plausibility of miracles produced by humans is answered by Jesus' own admission of the human impossibility of miracles, which are acts of God that are "impossible for men", but "with God all things are possible". (
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+19%3A26&amp;version=KJV%3BNIV" rel="nofollow">
        Matthew 19:26
       </a>
      </span>
      )
     </p>
     <p>
      The Roman Catholic Church and the Greek Orthodox Church reject Hume's argument against miracles outright with the teachings of St. Gregory Palamas, who postulated that Reason alone was not sufficient to understand God's energies (activities such as miracles) and essence, but faith was.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Miraculous healings through prayers, often involving the "
      <a href="/wiki/Laying_on_of_hands" title="Laying on of hands">
       laying on of hands
      </a>
      ", have been reported. However, reliance on
      <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">
       faith healing
      </a>
      alone can indirectly contribute to serious harm and even death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_apologetics" title="Christian apologetics">
       Christian apologists
      </a>
      including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.S._Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis">
       C.S. Lewis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Norman_Geisler" title="Norman Geisler">
       Norman Geisler
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/William_Lane_Craig" title="William Lane Craig">
       William Lane Craig
      </a>
      have argued that
      <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">
       miracles
      </a>
      are reasonable and plausible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">
       Ethics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ethics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Ethics_in_the_Bible" title="Ethics in the Bible">
       Ethics in the Bible
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Certain interpretations of some moral decisions in the Bible are considered
      <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">
       ethically
      </a>
      questionable by many modern groups. Some of the passages most commonly criticized include
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_colonialism" title="Christianity and colonialism">
       colonialism
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Christianity" title="Women in Christianity">
       subjugation of women
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_intolerance" title="Religious intolerance">
       religious intolerance
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_homosexuality" title="Christianity and homosexuality">
       condemnation of homosexuality
      </a>
      , and support for the institution of
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
       slavery
      </a>
      in both Old and New Testaments.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">
       philosopher
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">
       Friedrich Nietzsche
      </a>
      criticized the ethics of Christianity. See
      <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_Friedrich_Nietzsche#Christianity_and_morality" title="Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche">
       Philosophy of Friedrich Nietzsche#Christianity and morality
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Colonialism">
       Colonialism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Colonialism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_colonialism" title="Christianity and colonialism">
       Christianity and colonialism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_colonialism" title="Christianity and colonialism">
       Christianity and colonialism
      </a>
      are often closely associated because Catholicism, Russian Orthodoxy and Protestantism were the religions of the European colonial powers
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and acted in many ways as the "religious arm" of those powers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Initially, Christian missionaries were portrayed as "visible saints, exemplars of ideal piety in a sea of persistent savagery". However, by the time the colonial era drew to a close in the last half of the twentieth century, missionaries became viewed as ideological shock troops for colonial invasion whose zealotry blinded them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Christianity is targeted by critics of colonialism because the tenets of the religion were used to justify the actions of the colonists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, Michael Wood asserts that the indigenous peoples were not considered to be human beings and that the colonisers was shaped by "centuries of Ethnocentrism, and Christian monotheism, which espoused one truth, one time and version of reality."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Slavery">
       Slavery
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Slavery">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christianity_and_slavery" title="Christianity and slavery">
       Christianity and slavery
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">
       Early Christianity
      </a>
      variously opposed, accepted, or ignored slavery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Robinson-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The early Christian perspectives of slavery were formed in the contexts of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jewish_Christianity" title="Jewish Christianity">
       Christianity's roots in Judaism
      </a>
      , and as part of the wider culture of the
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
       Roman Empire
      </a>
      . Both the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testaments
      </a>
      recognize that the institution of slavery existed.
     </p>
     <p>
      The earliest surviving Christian teachings about slavery are from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saint_Paul" title="Saint Paul">
       Paul the Apostle
      </a>
      , who frequently referred to himself as a "Slave of Christ", perhaps implying that he was a slave and Jesus was his master, although it may have just been an expression. Paul did not renounce the institution of slavery. Conversely, he taught that Christian slaves ought to serve their masters wholeheartedly.
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Eph.+6%3A5-8&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Eph. 6:5-8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the same time, he taught slave owners to treat their slaves fairly. The entire
      <a href="/wiki/Epistle_to_Philemon" title="Epistle to Philemon">
       Epistle to Philemon
      </a>
      is devoted to
      <a href="/wiki/Onesimus" title="Onesimus">
       Onesimus
      </a>
      , a runaway slave and convert whom Paul returns to his master, to be seen as "not just a slave, but much more than a slave; he is a dear brother in Christ".
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Philemon+16&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Philemon 16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tradition describes
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Pius_I" title="Pope Pius I">
       Pope Pius I
      </a>
      (term c. 158-167) and
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Callixtus_I" title="Pope Callixtus I">
       Pope Callixtus I
      </a>
      (term c. 217-222) as former slaves.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since the Middle Ages, the
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christian
      </a>
      understanding of
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
       slavery
      </a>
      has been subjected to significant internal conflict and has endured dramatic change. Nearly all Christian leaders before the late 17th century recognised slavery, within specific biblical limitations, as consistent with Christian
      <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">
       theology
      </a>
      . The key verse used to justify slavery was Genesis 9:25-27: "Cursed be Canaan! The lowest of slaves will he be to his brothers. He also said, 'Blessed be the Lord, the God of Shem! May Canaan be the slave of Shem." which was interpreted to mean that Africans were the descendants of
      <a href="/wiki/Ham_(son_of_Noah)" title="Ham (son of Noah)">
       Ham
      </a>
      , cursed with "the mark of Ham" to be servants to the descendants of
      <a href="/wiki/Japheth" title="Japheth">
       Japheth
      </a>
      (Europeans) and
      <a href="/wiki/Shem" title="Shem">
       Shem
      </a>
      (Asians).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_54-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Robinson-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In early Medieval times, the Church discouraged slavery throughout Europe, largely eliminating it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostling_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ostling-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      That changed in 1452, when
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">
       Pope Nicholas V
      </a>
      instituted the hereditary slavery of captured
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslims
      </a>
      and pagans, regarding all non-Christians as "enemies of Christ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Rodney_Stark" title="Rodney Stark">
       Rodney Stark
      </a>
      makes the argument in
      <i>
       For the Glory of God: How Monotheism Led to Reformations, Science, Witch-Hunts, and the End of Slavery
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      that Christianity helped to end slavery worldwide, as does
      <a href="/wiki/Lamin_Sanneh" title="Lamin Sanneh">
       Lamin Sanneh
      </a>
      in
      <i>
       Abolitionists Abroad
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These authors point out that Christians who viewed slavery as wrong on the basis of their religious convictions spearheaded
      <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">
       abolitionism
      </a>
      , and many of the early campaigners for the abolition of slavery were driven by their Christian faith and a desire to realize their view that all people are equal under God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the late 17th century,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabaptist" title="Anabaptist">
       anabaptists
      </a>
      began to criticize slavery. Criticisms from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_Society_of_Friends" title="Religious Society of Friends">
       Society of Friends
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mennonite" title="Mennonite">
       Mennonites
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Amish" title="Amish">
       Amish
      </a>
      followed suit. Prominent among these Christian abolitionists were
      <a href="/wiki/William_Wilberforce" title="William Wilberforce">
       William Wilberforce
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Woolman" title="John Woolman">
       John Woolman
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Harriet_Beecher_Stowe" title="Harriet Beecher Stowe">
       Harriet Beecher Stowe
      </a>
      wrote her famous book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">
        Uncle Tom's Cabin
       </a>
      </i>
      , according to her Christian beliefs in 1852. Earlier, in Britain and America,
      <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">
       Quakers
      </a>
      were active in abolitionism. A group of Quakers founded the first English abolitionist organization, and a Quaker petition brought the issue before government that same year. The Quakers continued to be influential throughout the lifetime of the movement, in many ways leading the way for the campaign.
      <a href="/wiki/John_Wesley" title="John Wesley">
       John Wesley
      </a>
      , the founder of
      <a href="/wiki/Methodism" title="Methodism">
       Methodism
      </a>
      , was instrumental in starting abolitionism as a popular movement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many modern
      <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">
       Christians
      </a>
      are united in the condemnation of
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
       slavery
      </a>
      as wrong and contrary to
      <a href="/wiki/God" title="God">
       God
      </a>
      's will. Only peripheral groups such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">
       Ku Klux Klan
      </a>
      and other Christian
      <a href="/wiki/Hate_group" title="Hate group">
       hate groups
      </a>
      on the racist fringes of the
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_Reconstructionism" title="Christian Reconstructionism">
       Christian Reconstructionist
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_Identity" title="Christian Identity">
       Christian Identity
      </a>
      movements advocate the reinstitution of slavery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_54-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Robinson-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Full adherents to reconstructionism are few and marginalized among
      <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">
       conservative
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">
       Christians
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Martin1996a_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Martin1996a-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond1998p213_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Diamond1998p213-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OrtizKennedy_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OrtizKennedy-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With these exceptions, all Christian faith groups now condemn slavery, and see the practice as incompatible with basic Christian principles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Robinson_54-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Robinson-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ostling_56-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ostling-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to aiding
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Disputed statement">
         <span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (May 2013)">
          dubious
         </span>
        </a>
        <span class="metadata">
         
         <a href="/wiki/Talk:Criticism_of_Christianity#Dubious" title="Talk:Criticism of Christianity">
          discuss
         </a>
        </span>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      abolitionism, many Christians made further efforts toward establishing racial equality, contributing to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Movement" title="Civil Rights Movement">
       Civil Rights Movement
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       The African American Review
      </i>
      notes the important role Christian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Revivalism" title="Revivalism">
       revivalism
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Black_church" title="Black church">
       black church
      </a>
      played in the Civil Rights Movement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">
       Martin Luther King, Jr.
      </a>
      , an ordained
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baptist" title="Baptist">
       Baptist
      </a>
      minister, was a leader of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/American_Civil_Rights_Movement" title="American Civil Rights Movement">
       American Civil Rights Movement
      </a>
      and president of the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Christian_Leadership_Conference" title="Southern Christian Leadership Conference">
       Southern Christian Leadership Conference
      </a>
      , a Christian Civil Rights organization.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity_and_women">
       Christianity and women
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Christianity and women">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Women_in_Christianity" title="Women in Christianity">
       Women in Christianity
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Bible" title="Women in the Bible">
       Women in the Bible
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1482" data-file-width="980" height="333" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg/220px-Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg/330px-Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg/440px-Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Joan_of_arc_miniature_graded.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Joan_of_Arc" title="Joan of Arc">
         Joan of Arc
        </a>
        led battles in the fight to free France from England. She believed that God had commanded her to do so. Upon capture, she was tried for
        <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">
         heresy
        </a>
        by an English court and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burning_at_the_stake" title="Burning at the stake">
         burned at the stake
        </a>
        . She is now a
        <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">
         saint
        </a>
        venerated in the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
         Roman Catholic Church
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
         <a href="#cite_note-68">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          68
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many
      <a href="/wiki/Feminism" title="Feminism">
       feminists
      </a>
      have accused notions such as a male God, male prophets, and the man-centered stories in the Bible of contributing to a patriarchy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
