      Though many women disciples and servants are recorded in the
      <a href="/wiki/Pauline_epistles" title="Pauline epistles">
       Pauline epistles
      </a>
      , there have been occasions in which women have been denigrated and forced into a second-class status.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, women were told to keep silent in the churches for "it is a shame for a woman to speak in the church".
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1Co.+14%3A34-35&amp;version=NIV" rel="nofollow">
        [1Cor.14:34-35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_suffrage" title="Women's suffrage">
       Suffragist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Cady_Stanton" title="Elizabeth Cady Stanton">
       Elizabeth Cady Stanton
      </a>
      said in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Woman%27s_Bible" title="The Woman's Bible">
        The Woman's Bible
       </a>
      </i>
      that "the Bible in its teachings degrades women from Genesis to Revelation".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Elizabeth Clark cites early Christian writings by authors such as
      <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">
       Tertullian
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">
       Augustine
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/John_Chrysostom" title="John Chrysostom">
       John Chrysostom
      </a>
      as being exemplary of the negative view of women that has been perpetuated in church tradition.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Until the latter part of the 20th century, only the names of very few women who contributed to the formation of Christianity in its earliest years were widely known:
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_(mother_of_Jesus)" title="Mary (mother of Jesus)">
       Mary
      </a>
      , the mother of Jesus;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_Magdalene" title="Mary Magdalene">
       Mary Magdalene
      </a>
      , disciple of Jesus and the first witness to the resurrection; and
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_of_Bethany" title="Mary of Bethany">
       Mary
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Martha" title="Martha">
       Martha
      </a>
      , the sisters who offered him hospitality in Bethany.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-King-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">
       Harvard
      </a>
      scholar
      <a href="/wiki/Karen_Leigh_King" title="Karen Leigh King">
       Karen King
      </a>
      writes that more of the many women who contributed to the formation of Christianity in its earliest years are becoming known. Further, she concludes that for centuries in
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Christianity" title="Western Christianity">
       Western Christianity
      </a>
      , Mary Magdalene has been wrongly identified as the adulteress and repentant prostitute presented in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+8&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        John 8
       </a>
      </span>
      a connection supposed by tradition but nowhere claimed in the New Testament. According to King, the
      <a href="/wiki/Gospel_of_Mary" title="Gospel of Mary">
       Gospel of Mary
      </a>
      shows that she was an influential figure, a prominent disciple and leader of one wing of the early Christian movement that promoted women's leadership.
     </p>
     <p>
      King claims that every sect within
      <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">
       early Christianity
      </a>
      which had advocated women's prominence in ancient Christianity was eventually declared
      <a href="/wiki/Heresy_in_Christianity" title="Heresy in Christianity">
       heretical
      </a>
      , and evidence of women's early leadership roles was erased or suppressed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-King_74-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-King-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Classicist
      <a href="/wiki/Evelyn_Stagg" title="Evelyn Stagg">
       Evelyn Stagg
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      scholar
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Stagg_(theologian)" title="Frank Stagg (theologian)">
       Frank Stagg
      </a>
      in their jointly authored book,
      <i>
       Woman in the World of Jesus,
      </i>
      document very unfavorable attitudes toward women that prevailed in the world into which Jesus came. They assert that there is no recorded instance where Jesus disgraces, belittles, reproaches, or stereotypes a woman. They interpret the recorded treatment and attitude Jesus showed to women as evidence that the Founder of Christianity treated women with great dignity and respect.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Various theologians have concluded that the canonical examples of the manner of Jesus are instructive for inferring his attitudes toward women. They are seen as showing repeatedly and consistently how he liberated and affirmed women.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GB_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GB-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However,
      <a class="extiw" href="//de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schalom_Ben-Chorin" title="de:Schalom Ben-Chorin">
       Schalom Ben-Chorin
      </a>
      argues that Jesus' reply to his mother in
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=John+2%3A4&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        John 2:4
       </a>
      </span>
      during the
      <a href="/wiki/Marriage_at_Cana" title="Marriage at Cana">
       wedding at Cana
      </a>
      amounted to a blatant violation of the commandment to honor one's parent.
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Ex.+20%3A12&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Ex. 20:12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He mistakenly assumes Jesus' response to be an offensive statement, when in all actuality, the term "woman" was used to show respect in the Hebrew cultures. Also, Christ was an adult at the time, thirty years of age. He had the biblical right to refuse a command by his mother, and he did so stating that he was doing his Father's (God's) business.
     </p>
     <p>
      There are three major viewpoints within modern Christianity over the role of women. They are known respectively as
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_feminism" title="Christian feminism">
       Christian feminism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_egalitarianism" title="Christian egalitarianism">
       Christian egalitarianism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Complementarianism" title="Complementarianism">
       complementarianism
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       <i>
        Christian feminists
       </i>
       take a feminist position from a Christian perspective.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Christian egalitarians
       </i>
       advocate ability-based, rather than gender-based, ministry of Christians of all ages, ethnicities and socio-economic classes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
        <a href="#cite_note-79">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         79
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       Egalitarians support the
       <a href="/wiki/Ordination_of_women" title="Ordination of women">
        ordination of women
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Christian_views_on_marriage#Egalitarian_view" title="Christian views on marriage">
        equal roles
       </a>
       in marriage, but are theologically and morally more conservative than Christian feminists and prefer to avoid the label "feminist". A limited notion of gender complementarity is held by some, known as "complementarity without hierarchy".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DBE_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-DBE-80">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         80
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </li>
      <li>
       <i>
        Complementarians
       </i>
       support both equality and beneficial differences between men and women.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grudem_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Grudem-81">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         81
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       They believe the Bible teaches that men and women have distinct complementary roles in both marriage and in the church. They maintain that men have a responsibility to lead and women have a responsibility to submit to the leadership of men.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      Some Christians argue that the idea of God as a man is based less on gender but rather on the dominant Patriarchal society of the time in which men acted as leaders and caretakers of the Family.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Thus, the idea of God being "The Father" is with regards to his relationship with what are "his children", Christians.
     </p>
     <p>
      Most mainline Christians claim that the doctrine of the Trinity implies that God should be called Father and not called Mother, in the same way that Jesus was a man and was not a woman.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brumley_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brumley-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jesus tells His followers to address God as Father.
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mt.+6%3A9-13&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Mt. 6:9-13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He tells his disciples to be merciful as their heavenly Father is merciful.
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Lk.+6%3A36&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Lk. 6:36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He says the Father will give the Holy Spirit to those who ask
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Lk.+11%3A13&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Lk. 11:13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and that the Spirit of their Father will speak through them in times of persecution.
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Mt.+10%3A20&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Mt. 10:20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      On Easter Sunday, he directs Mary Magdalene to tell the other disciples, "I am going to my Father and your Father...."
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Jn.+20%3A17&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Jn. 20:17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mark Brumley points out that behind New Testament language of Divine Adoption and regeneration is the idea that God is our Father because He is the "source" or "origin" of our new life in Christ. He has saved us through Christ and sanctified us in the Spirit. Brumley claims this is clearly more than a metaphor; the analogy with earthly fatherhood is obvious. God is not merely like a father for Christs followers; he is really their Father. Among Christians who hold to this idea, there is a distinct sense that Jesus' treatment of women should imply equality in leadership and marital roles every bit as strongly as the definite male gender of Jesus should imply a name of Father for God. Rather than as antifeminist, they characterize alternative naming as unnecessary and unsupported by the words found in the Bible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brumley_83-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brumley-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2000, the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">
       Southern Baptist Convention
      </a>
      voted to revise its "Baptist Faith and Message" (Statement of Faith),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFM_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BFM-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      opposing women as pastors. While this decision is not binding and would not prevent women from serving as pastors, the revision itself has been criticized by some from within the convention. In the same document, the Southern Baptist Convention took a strong position of the subordinating view of woman in marriage: "A wife is to submit herself
      <i>
       graciously
      </i>
      to the servant leadership of her husband. She has the God-given responsibility to respect her husband and to serve as his helper in managing the household and nurturing the next generation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BFM_84-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-BFM-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (Emphasis added)
     </p>
     <p>
      The Eastern Orthodox Church does not allow female clergy. The Chaldean Catholic Church on the other hand continues to maintain a large number of deaconesses serving alongside male deacons during mass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In some evangelical churches, it is forbidden for women to become pastors, deacons or church elders. In support of such prohibitions, the verse
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=1tim+2%3A12&amp;version=KJV" rel="nofollow">
        1Timothy2:12
       </a>
      </span>
      is often cited:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       <i>
        But I suffer not a woman to teach, nor to usurp authority over the man, but to be in silence.
       </i>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity_and_politics">
       Christianity and politics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Christianity and politics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_politics" title="Christianity and politics">
       Christianity and politics
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_left" title="Christian left">
       Christian left
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_right" title="Christian right">
       Christian right
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religion_and_politics" title="Religion and politics">
       Religion and politics
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some
      <a href="/wiki/Left-wing_politics" title="Left-wing politics">
       leftists
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Libertarianism" title="Libertarianism">
       libertarians
      </a>
      , including Christians who disavow the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_Right" title="Religious Right">
       Religious Right
      </a>
      , use the term
      <i>
       Christian fascism
      </i>
      or
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Christofascism" title="Christofascism">
        Christofascism
       </a>
      </i>
      to describe what some see as an emerging
      <a href="/wiki/Neoconservatism" title="Neoconservatism">
       neoconservative
      </a>
      proto-fascism or
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_nationalism" title="Religious nationalism">
       Evangelical nationalism
      </a>
      and possible
      <a href="/wiki/Theocracy" title="Theocracy">
       theocratic
      </a>
      sentiment in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Reverend Rich Lang of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Methodist_Church" title="United Methodist Church">
       Trinity United Methodist Church
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">
       Seattle
      </a>
      gave a sermon titled "
      <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">
       George Bush
      </a>
      and the Rise of Christian Fascism", in which he said, "I want to flesh out the ideology of the Christian Fascism that Bush articulates. It is a form of Christianity that is the mirror opposite of what Jesus embodied."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Christianity_and_violence">
       Christianity and violence
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Christianity and violence">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_violence" title="Christianity and violence">
       Christianity and violence
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_terrorism" title="Christian terrorism">
       Christian terrorism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
       Crusades
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many critics of Christianity have cited the violent acts of Christian nations as a reason to denounce the religion. Science-fiction writer
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_C._Clarke" title="Arthur C. Clarke">
       Arthur C. Clarke
      </a>
      said that he could not forgive religions for the atrocities and wars over time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">
       Richard Dawkins
      </a>
      makes a similar case in his book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_God_Delusion" title="The God Delusion">
        The God Delusion
       </a>
      </i>
      . In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Dawkins_Delusion%3F" title="The Dawkins Delusion?">
        The Dawkins Delusion?
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">
       Alister McGrath
      </a>
      responds to Dawkins by suggesting that, far from endorsing "out-group hostility",
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      commanded an ethic of "out-group affirmation". McGrath agrees that it is necessary to critique religion, but says that Dawkins seems unaware that it possesses internal means of reform and renewal. While Christians may certainly be accused of failing to live up to Jesus' standard of acceptance, it is there at the heart of the Christian ethic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Peace, compassion and forgiveness of wrongs done by others are key elements of Christian teaching.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Christians have struggled since the days of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Church_fathers" title="Church fathers">
       Church fathers
      </a>
      with the question of when the use of force is justified.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Right_Reason_92-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Right_Reason-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Such debates have led to concepts such as
      <a href="/wiki/Just_war_theory" title="Just war theory">
       just war theory
      </a>
      . Throughout history, biblical passages have been used to justify the use of force against heretics,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      sinners
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and external enemies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Heitman and Hagan identify the
      <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">
       Inquisitions
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">
       Crusades
      </a>
      , wars of religion and
      <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">
       antisemitism
      </a>
      as being "among the most notorious examples of Christian violence".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      To this list, J. Denny Weaver adds, "warrior popes, support for
      <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">
       capital punishment
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Corporal_punishment" title="Corporal punishment">
       corporal punishment
      </a>
      under the guise of 'spare the rod and spoil the child', justifications of slavery, world-wide colonialism in the name of conversion to Christianity, the systemic violence of women subjected to men". Weaver employs a broader definition of violence that extends the meaning of the word to cover "harm or damage", not just physical violence per se. Thus, under his definition, Christian violence includes "forms of systemic violence such as poverty, racism, and sexism".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver_97-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weaver-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although some Christians have relied on Christian teaching to justify their use of force, other
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2013)">
          which?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Christians have opposed the use of force and violence. Some
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2013)">
          which?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      of the latter have formed sects that have emphasized pacificism as a central tenet of their faith.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Christians have also engaged in violence against those that they classify as heretics and non-believers. In
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Letter_to_a_Christian_Nation" title="Letter to a Christian Nation">
        Letter to a Christian Nation
       </a>
      </i>
      , critic of religion
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Harris_(author)" title="Sam Harris (author)">
       Sam Harris
      </a>
      writes that "...faith inspires violence in at least two ways. First, people often kill other human beings because they believe that the creator of the universe wants them to do it... Second, far greater numbers of people fall into conflict with one another because they define their moral community on the basis of their religious affiliation..."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SHarris_98-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SHarris-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Christian theologians point to a strong
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrinal" title="Doctrinal">
       doctrinal
      </a>
      and historical imperative within Christianity against violence, particularly Jesus'
      <a href="/wiki/Sermon_on_the_Mount" title="Sermon on the Mount">
       Sermon on the Mount
      </a>
      , which taught nonviolence and love of enemies. Weaver says that Jesus' pacifism was "preserved in the justifiable war doctrine that declares all war as sin even when declaring it occasionally a necessary evil, and in the prohibition of fighting by monastics and clergy as well as in a persistent tradition of
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_pacifism" title="Christian pacifism">
       Christian pacifism
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weaver_97-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Weaver-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Others point out sayings and acts of Jesus that do not fit this description: the absence of any censure of the soldier who asks Jesus to heal his servant, his overturning the tables and chasing the moneychangers from the temple with a rope in his hand, and through his Apostles, baptising a Roman Centurion who is never asked to first give up arms.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Addis_99-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Addis-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Science">
       Science
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Science">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="metadata plainlinks ambox ambox-content ambox-POV" role="presentation">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div style="width:52px">
         <img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" data-file-height="354" data-file-width="400" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" width="45"/>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         The
         <b>
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">
           neutrality
          </a>
          of this section is
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">
           disputed
          </a>
         </b>
         .
         <span class="hide-when-compact">
          Relevant discussion may be found on the
          <a href="/wiki/Talk:Criticism_of_Christianity#.23" title="Talk:Criticism of Christianity">
           talk page
          </a>
          . Please do not remove this message until the
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NPOV_dispute#What_is_an_NPOV_dispute.3F" title="Wikipedia:NPOV dispute">
           dispute is resolved
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <small>
          <i>
           (March 2013)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Science_and_the_Bible" title="Science and the Bible">
       Science and the Bible
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Relationship_between_religion_and_science" title="Relationship between religion and science">
       Relationship between religion and science
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      During the 19th century an interpretive model of the relationship between religion and science known today as the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conflict_theory" title="Conflict theory">
       conflict theory
      </a>
      developed, according to which interaction between religion and science almost inevitably leads to hostility and conflict. A popular example was the misconception that people from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Science in the Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      believed that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_Flat_Earth" title="Myth of the Flat Earth">
       Earth was flat
      </a>
      , and that only science, freed from religious dogma, had shown that it was spherical. This thesis was a popular
      <a href="/wiki/Historiography" title="Historiography">
       historiographical
      </a>
      approach during the late 19th and early 20th centuries, but most contemporary
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">
       historians of science
      </a>
      now reject it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shapin1996_101-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shapin1996-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brooke1991_102-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Brooke1991-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The notion of a war between science and religion remained common in the historiography of science during the late 19th and early 20th centuries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
       <a href="#cite_note-103">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        103
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most of today's historians of science consider that the conflict thesis has been superseded by subsequent historical research.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The framing of the relationship between Christianity and science as being predominantly one of conflict is still prevalent in
      <a href="/wiki/Popular_culture" title="Popular culture">
       popular culture
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The astronomer
      <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">
       Carl Sagan
      </a>
      , mentioned the dispute between the astronomical systems of
      <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
       Ptolemy
      </a>
      (who thought that the sun and planets revolved around the earth) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copernicus" title="Copernicus">
       Copernicus
      </a>
      (who thought the earth and planets revolved around the sun). He states in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cosmos:_A_Personal_Voyage" title="Cosmos: A Personal Voyage">
        Cosmos: A Personal Voyage
       </a>
      </i>
      that
      <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
       Ptolemy
      </a>
      's belief was "supported by the church through the Dark Ages [It] effectively prevented the advance of astronomy for 1,500 years."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106">
       <a href="#cite_note-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Ted_Peters_(theologian)" title="Ted Peters (theologian)">
       Ted Peters
      </a>
      in
      <i>
       Encyclopedia of Religion
      </i>
      writes that although there is some truth in this story, it has been exaggerated and has become "a modern myth perpetuated by those wishing to see warfare between science and religion who were allegedly persecuted by an atavistic and dogma-bound ecclesiastical authority".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1992, the
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
       Catholic Church
      </a>
      's seeming vindication of Galileo attracted much comment in the
      <a href="/wiki/Galileo_affair" title="Galileo affair">
       media
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Numerous scientists have criticized
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_fundamentalism" title="Christian fundamentalism">
       Christian fundamentalism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">
       creationism
      </a>
      as inherently unscientific and incompatible with modern understanding of
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_biology" title="Evolutionary biology">
       evolutionary biology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">
       geology
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">
       cosmology
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:God_the_Geometer.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1705" data-file-width="1244" height="302" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/God_the_Geometer.jpg/220px-God_the_Geometer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/God_the_Geometer.jpg/330px-God_the_Geometer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/God_the_Geometer.jpg/440px-God_the_Geometer.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:God_the_Geometer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Medieval scholars sought to understand the
        <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">
         geometric
        </a>
        and harmonic principles by which God created the universe.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geometer_110-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Geometer-110">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          110
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine">
       Doctrine
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Doctrine">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Incarnation">
       Incarnation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Incarnation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Incarnation_(Christianity)" title="Incarnation (Christianity)">
       Incarnation (Christianity)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The earliest objections to incarnation come from
      <a href="/wiki/Celsus" title="Celsus">
       Celsus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Porphyry_(philosopher)" title="Porphyry (philosopher)">
       Porphyry
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2010)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Celsus found it hard to reconcile Christian human God who was born and matured with his Jewish God who was supposed to be one and unchanging. He asked "if God wanted to reform humanity, why did he choose to descend and live on earth? How his brief presence in Jerusalem could benefit all the millions of people who lived elsewhere in the world or who had lived and died before his incarnation?"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One classical response is
      <a href="/wiki/Lewis%27s_trilemma" title="Lewis's trilemma">
       Lewis's trilemma
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Syllogism" title="Syllogism">
       syllogism
      </a>
      popularised by
      <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">
       C. S. Lewis
      </a>
      that intended to demonstrate the logical inconsistency of both holding
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      of Nazareth to be a "great moral teacher" while also denying his divinity. The logical soundness of this trilemma has been widely questioned.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hell_and_damnation">
       Hell and damnation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Hell and damnation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_Hell" title="Problem of Hell">
       Problem of Hell
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hell_in_Christianity" title="Hell in Christianity">
       Hell in Christianity
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adamandevesin.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3187" data-file-width="2347" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Adamandevesin.jpg/220px-Adamandevesin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Adamandevesin.jpg/330px-Adamandevesin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Adamandevesin.jpg/440px-Adamandevesin.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adamandevesin.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Adam_and_Eve" title="Adam and Eve">
         Adam and Eve
        </a>
        being driven from Eden due to original sin, portrayed by
        <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Dor%C3%A9" title="Gustave Dor">
         Gustave Dor
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Christianity has been criticized as seeking to persuade people into accepting its authority through simple fear of punishment or, conversely, through hope of reward after death, rather than through rational argumentation or
      <a href="/wiki/Empirical_evidence" title="Empirical evidence">
       empirical evidence
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Traditional Christian doctrine dictates that, without faith in Jesus Christ or in the Christian faith in general, one is subject to eternal punishment in
      <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">
       Hell
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Critics regard the eternal punishment of those who fail to adopt Christian faith as morally objectionable, and consider it an abhorrent picture of the nature of the world. On a similar theme objections are made against the perceived injustice of punishing a person for all eternity for a temporal crime. Some Christians agree (see
      <a href="/wiki/Annihilationism" title="Annihilationism">
       Annihilationism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Trinitarian_Universalism" title="Trinitarian Universalism">
       Trinitarian Universalism
      </a>
      ). These beliefs have been considered especially repugnant
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
       <a href="#cite_note-115">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        115
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      when the claimed omnipotent God makes, or allows a person to come into existence, with a nature that desires that which God finds objectionable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116">
       <a href="#cite_note-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the Abrahamic religions,
      <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">
       Hell
      </a>
      has traditionally been regarded as a
      <a href="/wiki/Punishment" title="Punishment">
       punishment
      </a>
      for wrongdoing or
      <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">
       sin
      </a>
      in this life, as a manifestation of divine justice. As in the
      <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">
       problem of evil
      </a>
      , some apologists argue that the torments of Hell are attributable not to a defect in God's benevolence, but in human
      <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">
       free will
      </a>
      . Although a benevolent God would prefer to see everyone saved, he would also allow humans to control their own destinies. This view opens the possibility of seeing Hell not as retributive punishment, but rather as an option that God allows, so that people who do not wish to be with God are not forced to be.
      <a href="/wiki/C._S._Lewis" title="C. S. Lewis">
       C. S. Lewis
      </a>
      most famously proposed this view in his book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Great_Divorce" title="The Great Divorce">
        The Great Divorce
       </a>
      </i>
      , saying: "There are only two kinds of people in the end: those who say to God, 'Thy will be done,' and those to whom God says, in the end, 'Thy will be done.'"
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Hell" title="Hell">
       Hell
      </a>
      is not seen as strictly a matter of retributive justice even by the more traditionalist churches. For example, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">
       Eastern Orthodox
      </a>
      see it as a condition brought about by, and the natural consequence of, free rejection of God's love.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">
       Roman Catholic Church
      </a>
      teaches that hell is a place of punishment
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      brought about by a person's self exclusion from communion with God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In some ancient Eastern Orthodox traditions, Hell and Heaven are distinguished not spatially, but by the relation of a person to God's love.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some modern critics of the doctrine of Hell (such as
      <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_McCord_Adams" title="Marilyn McCord Adams">
       Marilyn McCord Adams
      </a>
      ) claim that, even if Hell is seen as a choice rather than as punishment, it would be unreasonable for God to give such flawed and ignorant creatures as humans the awesome responsibility of their eternal destinies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Kvanvig" title="Jonathan Kvanvig">
       Jonathan Kvanvig
      </a>
      , in his book,
      <i>
       The Problem of Hell
      </i>
      , agrees that God would not allow one to be eternally damned by a decision made under the wrong circumstances. For instance, one should not always honor the choices of human beings, even when they are full adults, if, for instance, the choice is made while
      <a href="/wiki/Depression_(mood)" title="Depression (mood)">
       depressed
      </a>
      or careless. On Kvanvig's view, God will abandon no person until they have made a settled, final decision, under favorable circumstances, to reject God, but God will respect a choice made under the right circumstances. Once a person finally and competently chooses to reject God, out of respect for the person's autonomy, God allows them to be annihilated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Idolatry">
       Idolatry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Idolatry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Despite Christians usually alleges different religions to be idolatrous, they have been pointed out by number of notable people to have been engaged in idolatry, the common practices of Christians that have been regarded as idolatry contains the use of images of Jesus, Mary, Saints, etc.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Limbo">
       Limbo
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Limbo">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Roman Catholic Church teaches that
      <a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">
       baptism
      </a>
      is a necessity. In the 5th century,
      <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">
       St. Augustine
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo#Sacramental_theology" title="Augustine of Hippo">
       concluded
      </a>
      that infants who die without baptism were consigned to hell.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the 13th century, theologians referred to the "
      <a href="/wiki/Limbo" title="Limbo">
       limbo
      </a>
      of infants" as a place where unbaptized babies were deprived of the vision of God, but did not suffer because they did not know of that which they were deprived, and moreover enjoyed perfect natural happiness. The 1983 Code of Canon Law (1183 2) specifies that
      <i>
       "Children whose parents had intended to have them baptized but who died before baptism, may be allowed church funeral rites by the local ordinary"
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2007, the 30-member International Theological Commission revisited the concept of limbo.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-catholicnews.com_125-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-catholicnews.com-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the commission also said that hopefulness was not the same as certainty about the destiny of such infants.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-catholicnews.com_125-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-catholicnews.com-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rather, as stated in the Catechism of the Catholic Church, 1257, "God has bound salvation to the sacrament of Baptism, but he himself is not bound by his sacraments."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hope in the mercy of God is not the same as certainty through the sacraments, but it is not without result, as demonstrated in Jesus' statement to the thief on the cross in Luke 23:42-43.
     </p>
     <p>
      The concept of limbo is not accepted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">
       Orthodox Church
      </a>
      or by
      <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">
       Protestants
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Atonement">
       Atonement
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Atonement">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The idea of
      <a href="/wiki/Atonement_in_Christianity" title="Atonement in Christianity">
       atonement
      </a>
      for
      <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">
       sin
      </a>
      is criticized by
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">
       Richard Dawkins
      </a>
      on the grounds that the image of God as requiring the suffering and death of Jesus to effect reconciliation with humankind is immoral. The view is summarized by Dawkins: "if God wanted to forgive our sins, why not just forgive them? Who is God trying to impress?"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Oxford theologian Alister McGrath maintains that Dawkins is "ignorant" of
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_theology" title="Christian theology">
       Christian theology
      </a>
      , and therefore unable to engage religion and faith intelligently. He goes on to say that the atonement was necessary because of our flawed human nature, which made it impossible for us to save ourselves, and that it expresses God's love for us by removing the sin that stands in the way of our reconciliation with God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Responding to the criticism that he is "ignorant" of theology, Dawkins asks, "Do you have to read up on leprechology before disbelieving in
      <a href="/wiki/Leprechaun" title="Leprechaun">
       leprechauns
      </a>
      ?"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and "[y]es, I have, of course, met this point before. It sounds superficially fair. But it presupposes that there is something in Christian theology to be ignorant about. The entire thrust of my position is that Christian theology is a non-subject."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Dinesh_D%27Souza" title="Dinesh D'Souza">
       Dinesh D'Souza
      </a>
      says that Dawkins' criticism "only makes sense if you assume Christians made the whole thing up." He goes on to say that Christians view it as a beautiful sacrifice, and that "through the extremity of Golgotha, Christ reconciles divine justice and divine mercy."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Andrew Wilson argues that Dawkins misses the point of the atonement, which has nothing to do with masochism, but is based on the concepts of holiness, sin and grace.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_G._Ingersoll" title="Robert G. Ingersoll">
       Robert Green Ingersoll
      </a>
      suggests that the concept of the atonement is simply an extension of the Mosaic tradition of blood sacrifice and "is the enemy of morality".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The death of Jesus Christ represents the blood sacrifice to end all blood sacrifices; the resulting mechanism of atonement by proxy through that final sacrifice has appeal as a more convenient and much less costly approach to redemption than repeated animal sacrificea common sense solution to the problem of reinterpreting ancient religious approaches based on sacrifice.
     </p>
     <p>
      The prominent Christian apologist
      <a href="/wiki/Josh_McDowell" title="Josh McDowell">
       Josh McDowell
      </a>
      , in
      <i>
       More Than A Carpenter
      </i>
      , addresses the issue through an analogy of a real-life judge in California who was forced to fine his daughter $100 for speeding, but then came down, took off his robe, and paid the fine for her from his billfold,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though as in this and other cases, illustrations are only cautiously intended to describe certain aspects of the atonement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Second_Coming">
       Second Coming
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Second Coming">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">
       Second Coming
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Several verses in the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">
       New Testament
      </a>
      appear to contain
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      ' predictions that the
      <a href="/wiki/Second_Coming" title="Second Coming">
       Second Coming
      </a>
      would take place within a century following his death.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jesus appears to promise for his followers the second coming to happen before the generation he is preaching to vanishes. This is seen as an essential failure in the teachings of Christ by many critics such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">
       Bertrand Russell
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preterist" title="Preterist">
       Preterists
      </a>
      argue that Jesus did not mean his second coming
      <sup class="plainlinks nourlexpansion reference">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matt.+16%3A28&amp;version=ESV" rel="nofollow">
        [Matt. 16:28]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but speaks about demonstrations of his might, formulating this as "coming in his kingdom", especially the destruction of the Jerusalem Temple 70 AD, which he foretold, and by which time not all of his disciples were still living.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to this view
      <span class="nourlexpansion nowrap">
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.biblegateway.com/passage/?search=Matthew+10%3A23&amp;version=31" rel="nofollow">
        Matthew 10:23
       </a>
      </span>
      should be understood in the same way.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Inconsistency_with_Old_Testament_conception_of_the_afterlife">
       Inconsistency with Old Testament conception of the afterlife
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Inconsistency with Old Testament conception of the afterlife">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Afterlife#Christianity" title="Afterlife">
       Afterlife  Christianity
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Most Christian traditions teach belief in life after death as a central and indispensable tenet of their faith. Critics argue that the Christian conception of the afterlife is inconsistent with that described in the
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Testament" title="Old Testament">
       Old Testament
      </a>
      . George E. Mendenhall believes there is no concept of immortality or life after death in the Old Testament.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_143-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The presumption is that the deceased are inert, lifeless, and engaging in no activity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_143-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, two men, Enoch and Elijah, are taken into the afterlife without ever experiencing death.
     </p>
     <p>
      The idea of
      <a href="/wiki/Sheol" title="Sheol">
       Sheol
      </a>
      ("") or a state of nothingness was shared among
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_mythology" title="Babylonian mythology">
       Babylonian
      </a>
      and Israelite beliefs. "
      <a href="/wiki/Sheol" title="Sheol">
       Sheol
      </a>
      , as it was called by the ancient Israelites, is the land of no return, lying below the cosmic ocean, to which all, the mighty and the weak, travel in the ghostly form they assume after death, known as Raphraim. There the dead have no experience of either joy or pain, perceiving no light, feeling no movement."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiply_144-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-multiply-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Obayashi alludes that the Israelites were satisfied with such a shadowy realm of afterlife because they were more deeply concerned with survival.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiply_144-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-multiply-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Before Christianity began in the 1st century, the belief in an afterlife was already prevalent in
      <a href="/wiki/Jewish_eschatology" title="Jewish eschatology">
       Jewish thinking
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewish_145-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jewish-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      among the
      <a href="/wiki/Pharisees" title="Pharisees">
       Pharisees
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pharisees_147-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-pharisees-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Essenes" title="Essenes">
       Essenes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-essenes_148-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-essenes-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The themes of unity and
      <a href="/wiki/Sheol" title="Sheol">
       sheol
      </a>
      which largely shaped the ancient tradition of Judaism had been undermined when only the most pious of Jews were being massacred during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maccabean" title="Maccabean">
       Maccabean
      </a>
      revolt.
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_of_Christians">
       Criticism of Christians
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Criticism of Christians">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-Christian_sentiment" title="Anti-Christian sentiment">
       Anti-Christian sentiment
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Christian_right">
       Christian right
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Christian right">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_right" title="Christian right">
       Conservative Christians
      </a>
      are often accused of being intolerant by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular_humanists" title="Secular humanists">
       secular humanists
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">
       liberal Christians
      </a>
      , claiming that they oppose science that seems to contradict scripture (
      <a href="/wiki/Creationism" title="Creationism">
       Creationism
      </a>
      , use of
      <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">
       birth control
      </a>
      , research into
      <a href="/wiki/Embryonic_stem_cell" title="Embryonic stem cell">
       embryonic stem cells
      </a>
      , etc.),
      <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">
       liberal democracy
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_church_and_state" title="Separation of church and state">
       separation of church and state
      </a>
      ), and progressive social policies (rights of people of other races and religions, of women, and of people with different sexual orientations).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-berlet1998_149-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-berlet1998-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbcjuly2001_151-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-bbcjuly2001-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-white_152-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-white-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">
       United States
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: United States">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Gallup polling shows that within the US, trust in organized religion has declined since the 70's.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
       <a href="#cite_note-153">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        153
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Phil Zuckerman, a sociology professor, argues that political campaigning against same-sex marriage in churches is turning off so many people from Christianity, and responsible for a decline in the number of Christians in the United States.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      David Kinnaman, president of the
      <a href="/wiki/George_Barna" title="George Barna">
       Barna Institute
      </a>
      , and Gabe Lyons of the Fermi Project published a study of attitudes of 16- to 29-year-old Americans towards Christianity. They found that about 38% of all those who were not regular churchgoers had negative impressions of Christianity, and especially evangelical Christianity, associating it with conservative political activism, hypocrisy, anti-homosexuality,
      <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">
       authoritarianism
      </a>
      , and judgmentalism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      About 17% had "very bad" perceptions of Christianity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156">
       <a href="#cite_note-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157">
       <a href="#cite_note-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158">
       <a href="#cite_note-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hypocrisy">
       Hypocrisy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Hypocrisy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Proper_hands.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="914" data-file-width="525" height="383" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8c/Proper_hands.jpg/220px-Proper_hands.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8c/Proper_hands.jpg/330px-Proper_hands.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8c/Proper_hands.jpg/440px-Proper_hands.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Proper_hands.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Protestant Christian dominated KKK hinting at violence toward Jews and Catholics. Illustration by Rev.
        <a href="/wiki/Branford_Clarke" title="Branford Clarke">
         Branford Clarke
        </a>
        from
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Heroes_of_the_Fiery_Cross" title="Heroes of the Fiery Cross">
          Heroes of the Fiery Cross
         </a>
        </i>
        1928 by Bishop
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alma_White" title="Alma White">
         Alma White
        </a>
        published by the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pillar_of_Fire_Church" title="Pillar of Fire Church">
         Pillar of Fire Church
        </a>
        in
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zarephath,_NJ" title="Zarephath, NJ">
         Zarephath, NJ
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gaudium_et_spes" title="Gaudium et spes">
        Gaudium et spes
       </a>
      </i>
      claims that the example of Christians may be a contributory factor to
      <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">
       atheism
      </a>
      , writing, "believers can have more than a little to do with the birth of atheism. To the extent that they neglect their own training in the faith, or teach erroneous doctrine, or are deficient in their religious, moral, or social life, they must be said to conceal rather than reveal the authentic face of God and religion."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Secular and religious critics have accused many Christians of being hypocritical.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160">
       <a href="#cite_note-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Tom Whiteman, a Philadelphia psychologist found that the primary reasons for Christian divorce include adultery, abuse (including substance, physical and verbal abuse), and abandonment whereas the number one reason cited for divorce in the general population was incompatibility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Materialism">
       Materialism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Materialism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
