     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       <p>
        Instead of understanding and following the teachings of Jesus, the Christians argued and quarreled about the nature of Jesuss divinity and about the Trinity. They called each other heretics and persecuted each other and cut each others heads off. There was a great and violent controversy at one time among different Christian sects over a certain diphthong. One party said that the word Homo-ousion should be used in a prayer; the other wanted Homoi-ousion-this difference had reference to the divinity of Jesus. Over this diphthong fierce war was raged and large numbers of people were slaughtered.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: right;">
        
        <a href="/wiki/Jawaharlal_Nehru" title="Jawaharlal Nehru">
         Jawaharlal Nehru
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
         <a href="#cite_note-162">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          162
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
         <a href="#cite_note-163">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          163
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       <p>
        I like your Christ. I do not like your Christians. Your Christians are so unlike your Christ. The materialism of affluent Christian countries appears to contradict the claims of Jesus Christ that says it's not possible to worship both Mammon and God at the same time.
       </p>
       <p style="text-align: right;">
        
        <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">
         Mahatma Gandhi
        </a>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164">
         <a href="#cite_note-164">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          164
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </p>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sectarianism">
       Sectarianism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Sectarianism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Sectarianism" title="Sectarianism">
       Sectarianism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">
       Christian denomination
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some have argued that Christianity is undermined by the inability of Christians to agree on matters of faith and church governance, and the tendency for the content of their faith to be determined by regional or political factors.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schopenhauer" title="Schopenhauer">
       Schopenhauer
      </a>
      sarcastically suggested:
     </p>
     <dl>
      <dd>
       To the South German ecclesiastic the truth of the Catholic dogma is quite obvious, to the North German, the Protestant. If then, these convictions are based on objective reasons, the reasons must be climatic, and thrive, like plants, some only here, some only there. The convictions of those who are thus locally convinced are taken on trust and believed by the masses everywhere.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiple29_165-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-multiple29-165">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         165
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </dd>
     </dl>
     <p>
      Christians respond that
      <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">
       Ecumenism
      </a>
      has helped bring together such communities, where in the past mistranslations of Christological Greek terms may have resulted in seemingly different views.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-denominational_Christianity" title="Non-denominational Christianity">
       Non-denominational Christianity
      </a>
      represents another approach towards reducing the divisions within Christianity, although many Christian groups claiming to be non-denominational wind up with similar problems.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Persecution_by_Christians">
       Persecution by Christians
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Persecution by Christians">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_debate_on_persecution_and_toleration" title="Christian debate on persecution and toleration">
       Christian debate on persecution and toleration
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_violence" title="Christianity and violence">
       Christianity and violence
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Individuals and groups throughout history have been persecuted by certain Christians (and Christian groups) based upon sex, sexual orientation, race, and religion (even within the bounds of Christianity itself). Many of the persecutors attempted to justify their actions with particular scriptural interpretations. During
      <a href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">
       Late Antiquity
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      , important Christian theologians advocated
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_persecution" title="Religious persecution">
       religious persecution
      </a>
      to varying degrees.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2009)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      However,
      <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">
       Early modern Europe
      </a>
      witnessed a turning point in the Christian debate on persecution and toleration. Nowadays all significant
      <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">
       Christian denominations
      </a>
      embrace
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" title="Religious toleration">
       religious toleration
      </a>
      , and "look back on centuries of persecution with a mixture of revulsion and incomprehension".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">
       Early Christianity
      </a>
      was a minority religion in the
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">
       Roman Empire
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persecution_of_early_Christians_by_the_Romans" title="Persecution of early Christians by the Romans">
       the early Christians were themselves persecuted during that time
      </a>
      . After
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Constantine_I_and_Christianity" title="Constantine I and Christianity">
       Constantine I converted to Christianity
      </a>
      , it became the dominant religion in the Roman Empire. Already under the reign of Constantine I, Christian heretics had been persecuted; beginning in the late 4th century AD also the ancient pagan religions were actively suppressed. In the view of many historians, the
      <a href="/wiki/Constantinian_shift" title="Constantinian shift">
       Constantinian shift
      </a>
      turned Christianity from a persecuted into a persecuting religion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">
       decline of the Roman Empire
      </a>
      , the further
      <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">
       Christianization
      </a>
      of Europe was to a large extent peaceful.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, encounters between
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_Paganism" title="Christianity and Paganism">
       Christians and Pagans
      </a>
      were sometimes confrontational, and some Christian
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King" title="King">
       kings
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">
       Charlemagne
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olaf_I_of_Norway" title="Olaf I of Norway">
       Olaf I of Norway
      </a>
      ) were known for their violence against pagans. In the late Middle Ages, the appearance of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathar" title="Cathar">
       Cathars
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bogomils" title="Bogomils">
       Bogomils
      </a>
      in Europe laid the stage for the later
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Witch-hunts" title="Witch-hunts">
       witch-hunts
      </a>
      . These (probably
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gnostic" title="Gnostic">
       gnostic
      </a>
      -influenced) sects were seen as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heretics" title="Heretics">
       heretics
      </a>
      by the
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">
       Catholic Church
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Inquisition" title="Inquisition">
       Inquisition
      </a>
      was established to counter them.
     </p>
     <p>
      After the
      <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" title="Protestant Reformation">
       Protestant Reformation
      </a>
      , the devastation caused by the partly religiously motivated wars (
      <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">
       Thirty Years' War
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">
       English Civil War
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/French_Wars_of_Religion" title="French Wars of Religion">
       French Wars of Religion
      </a>
      ) in Europe in the 17th century gave rise to the ideas of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Religious_toleration" title="Religious toleration">
       Religious toleration
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Freedom_of_religion" title="Freedom of religion">
       Freedom of religion
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">
       Religious pluralism
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Response_of_apologists">
       Response of apologists
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Response of apologists">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Christians will sometimes point out that in their points of view, the wrongdoings of other Christians are not the fault of their religious scriptures but of those who have wrongly interpreted it. They posit that the mistakes of Christians do not refute the validity of their teachings, but merely proves their weakness and sinful nature, of which they then turn to Christ. Thus, according to them, the "Word of God" can still be true and valid without it having been accurately followed.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      According to
      <a href="/wiki/Ron_Sider" title="Ron Sider">
       Ron Sider
      </a>
      , an Evangelical theologian, "The tragedy is that poll after poll by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gallup_poll" title="Gallup poll">
       Gallup
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/The_Barna_Group" title="The Barna Group">
       Barna
      </a>
      show that evangelicals live just like the world. Contrast that with what the New Testament says about what happens when people come to living faith in Christ. There's supposed to be radical transformation in the power of the Holy Spirit (2 Cor 5:17, 1 Cor 10:13). The disconnect between our biblical beliefs and our practice is just, I think, heart-rending."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Similar arguments are held by Roman Catholics against critics of the Catholic Church, or by other Christians defending their respective Churches.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2008)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      of the Church's structure. Roman Catholics will argue that Popes who were corrupt in the Middle Ages is not the fault of the position of the Papacy or of the fact that there are obedient Priests lower in the hierarchy, but the fault of the individual people who act as "God's representative on Earth". Such examples can be seen in
      <a href="/wiki/Dante_Alighieri" title="Dante Alighieri">
       Dante
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Divine_Comedy" title="Divine Comedy">
        Divine Comedy
       </a>
      </i>
      , where Roman Catholic Clergy who had practiced
      <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">
       simony
      </a>
      find themselves in the lower circles of hell.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anti-clericalism_in_Nazi_Germany">
       Anti-clericalism in Nazi Germany
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Anti-clericalism in Nazi Germany">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Anti-clericalism" title="Anti-clericalism">
       Anti-clericalism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Nazi_Germany" title="Religion in Nazi Germany">
       Religion in Nazi Germany
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Adolf_Hitler-1933.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="328" data-file-width="227" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Adolf_Hitler-1933.jpg/170px-Adolf_Hitler-1933.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Adolf_Hitler-1933.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/66/Adolf_Hitler-1933.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Adolf_Hitler-1933.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">
         Adolf Hitler
        </a>
        established the
        <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reich_Church" title="Protestant Reich Church">
         Protestant Reich Church
        </a>
        and suppressed dissenting
        <a href="/wiki/Confessing_Church" title="Confessing Church">
         Confessing Churches
        </a>
        .
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382_170-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382-170">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          170
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitler_p219_171-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-Hitler_p219-171">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          171
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A,_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="572" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A%2C_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg/170px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A%2C_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A%2C_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg/255px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A%2C_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A%2C_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg/340px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A%2C_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_146-1968-101-20A,_Joseph_Goebbels.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">
         Joseph Goebbels
        </a>
        , as the
        <a href="/wiki/Reichs_Ministry_of_Public_Enlightenment_and_Propaganda" title="Reichs Ministry of Public Enlightenment and Propaganda">
         Reich Minister of Propaganda
        </a>
        worked to implement
        <a href="/wiki/Kirchenkampf" title="Kirchenkampf">
         Kirchenkampf
        </a>
        (church struggle), limiting the power of independent churches. He wrote that there was "an insoluble opposition between the Christian and a heroic-German world view".
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382_170-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382-170">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          170
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Adolf Hitler's 1920
      <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Program" title="National Socialist Program">
       Nazi Party Platform
      </a>
      promoted
      <a href="/wiki/Positive_Christianity" title="Positive Christianity">
       Positive Christianity
      </a>
      - which mixed ideas of racial purity and Nazi ideology with elements of Christianity, and removed "Jewish" elements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-William-L-Shirer-pp238-9_172-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-William-L-Shirer-pp238-9-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Nazism aimed to transform the subjective consciousness of the German peopletheir attitudes, values and mentalitiesinto a single-minded, obedient "national community". The Nazis believed they would therefore have to replace class, religious and regional allegiances.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Under the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gleichschaltung" title="Gleichschaltung">
        Gleichschaltung
       </a>
      </i>
      process, Hitler attempted to create a unified
      <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reich_Church" title="Protestant Reich Church">
       Protestant Reich Church
      </a>
      from Germany's 28 existing Protestant churches. The plan failed, and was resisted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Confessing_Church" title="Confessing Church">
       Confessing Church
      </a>
      . Persecution of the
      <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Germany" title="Roman Catholicism in Germany">
       Catholic Church in Germany
      </a>
      followed the Nazi takeover. Hitler moved quickly to eliminate
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_catholicism" title="Political catholicism">
       political catholicism
      </a>
      . Amid harassment of the Church, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Reich_concordat" title="Reich concordat">
       Reich concordat
      </a>
      treaty with the Vatican was signed in 1933, and promised to respect Church autonomy. Hitler routinely disregarded the Concordat, closing all Catholic institutions whose functions were not strictly religious. Clergy, nuns, and lay leaders were targeted, with thousands of arrests over the ensuing years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_Britannica_Online_2013_175-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica_Online_2013-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Hitler was supportive of Christianity in public, yet hostile to it in private. Anti-clericalists like
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Goebbels" title="Joseph Goebbels">
       Joseph Goebbels
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bormann" title="Martin Bormann">
       Martin Bormann
      </a>
      saw the conflict with the Churches as a priority concern, and anti-church and anti-clerical sentiments were strong among grassroots party activists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382_170-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hitler was born to a practising Catholic mother and an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticlerical" title="Anticlerical">
       anticlerical
      </a>
      father, but after leaving home Hitler never again attended
      <a href="/wiki/Mass_(liturgy)" title="Mass (liturgy)">
       Mass
      </a>
      or received the
      <a href="/wiki/Sacraments_of_the_Catholic_Church" title="Sacraments of the Catholic Church">
       sacraments
      </a>
      . According to biographer Alan Bullock, Hitler retained some regard for the organisational power of Catholicism but held private contempt for its central teachings, which he said, if taken to their conclusion, "would mean the systematic cultivation of the human failure.":
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hitler_p219_171-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hitler_p219-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Joseph Goebbels, the
      <a href="/wiki/Reichs_Ministry_of_Public_Enlightenment_and_Propaganda" title="Reichs Ministry of Public Enlightenment and Propaganda">
       Reich Minister of Propaganda
      </a>
      used his position to widely publicise trials of clergy and nuns in his propaganda campaigns, showing the cases in the worst possible light. In 1928, soon after his election to the Reichstag, Goebbels wrote in his diary that National Socialism was a "religion" that needed a genius to uproot "outmoded religious practices" and put new ones in their place: "One day soon National Socialism will be the religion of all Germans. My Party is my church, and I believe I serve the Lord best if I do his will, and liberate my oppressed people from the fetters of slavery. That is my gospel."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As the war progressed, on the "Church Question", he wrote "after the war it has to be generally solved... There is, namely, an insoluble opposition between the Christian and a heroic-German world view".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382_170-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ian_Kershaw_p.381-382-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Hitler's chosen deputy and private secretary,
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bormann" title="Martin Bormann">
       Martin Bormann
      </a>
      , was a rigid guardian of National Socialist orthodoxy and saw Christianity and Nazism as "incompatible" (mainly because of its Jewish origins),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Encyclopedia_Britannica_Online_2013_175-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Encyclopedia_Britannica_Online_2013-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as did the official Nazi philosopher,
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Rosenberg" title="Alfred Rosenberg">
       Alfred Rosenberg
      </a>
      . In his "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myth_of_the_Twentieth_Century" title="Myth of the Twentieth Century">
       Myth of the Twentieth Century
      </a>
      " (1930), Rosenberg wrote that the main enemies of the Germans were the "Russian Tartars" and "Semites" - with "Semites" including Christians, especially the Catholic Church.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      According to
      <a href="/wiki/Alan_Bullock" title="Alan Bullock">
       Bullock
      </a>
      , Hitler considered the Protestant clergy to be "insignificant" and "submissive" and lacking in a religion to be taken seriously.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
       <a href="#cite_note-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hitler attempted to create a unified
      <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reich_Church" title="Protestant Reich Church">
       Protestant Reich Church
      </a>
      from 28 separate regional churches through
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Gleichschaltung" title="Gleichschaltung">
        Gleichschaltung
       </a>
      </i>
      . His bid to create a unified Reich Church ultimately failed, and Hitler became disinterested in supporting the so-called "
      <a href="/wiki/German_Christians" title="German Christians">
       German Christians
      </a>
      " Nazi aligned movement. Hitler initially lent support to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ludwig_Muller" title="Ludwig Muller">
       Ludwig Muller
      </a>
      , a Nazi and former naval chaplain, to serve as Reich Bishop, but his heretical views against Paul the Apostle and the Semitic origins of Christ and the Bible (see
      <a href="/wiki/Positive_Christianity" title="Positive Christianity">
       Positive Christianity
      </a>
      ) quickly alienated sections of the Protestant church. Lutheran Pastor
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Neimoller" title="Martin Neimoller">
       Martin Neimoller
      </a>
      created the
      <a href="/wiki/Confessing_Church" title="Confessing Church">
       Confessing Church
      </a>
      movement to oppose the Nazification of protestant churches.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Neimoller was arrested by the Gestapo in 1937, and sent to the concentration camps.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
       <a href="#cite_note-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Confessing Church seminary was prohibited that same year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism_by_other_religions">
       Criticism by other religions
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Criticism by other religions">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">
       Hinduism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Hinduism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG/220px-Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG/330px-Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG/440px-Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ram_Mohan_Roy.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Statue of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Raja_Ram_Mohan_Roy" title="Raja Ram Mohan Roy">
         Raja Ram Mohan Roy
        </a>
        at College Green, Bristol,
        <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">
         UK
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ram_Mohan_Roy" title="Ram Mohan Roy">
       Ram Mohan Roy
      </a>
      criticized Christian doctrines, and asserted that how "unreasonable" and "self-contradictory" they are.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He further adds that people, even from India were embracing Christianity due to the economic hardship and weakness, just like European Jews were pressured to embrace Christianity, by both encouragement and force.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vivekananda" title="Vivekananda">
       Vivekananda
      </a>
      regarded Christianity as "..collection of little bits of Indian thought. Ours is the religion of which Buddhism with all its greatness is a rebel child, and of which Christianity is a very patchy imitation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Philosopher
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dayanand_Saraswati" title="Dayanand Saraswati">
       Dayanand Saraswati
      </a>
      , regarded Christianity as "barbarous religion, and a 'false religion' religion believed only by fools and by the people in a state of barbarism,"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      he included that Bible contains many stories and precepts that are immoral, praising cruelty, deceit and encouraging sin.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Highly acclaimed
      <a href="/wiki/Sarvepalli_Radhakrishnan" title="Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan">
       Sarvepalli Radhakrishnan
      </a>
      , writes:-
     </p>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       Unfortunately Christian religion inherited the Semitic creed of the jealous God in the view of Christ as the only begotten son of God so could not brook any rival near the throne. When Europe accepted the Christian religion, in spite of its own broad humanism, it accepted the fierce intolerance which is the natural result of belief in 'the truth once for all delivered to the saints.'
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
        <a href="#cite_note-188">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         188
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Judaism">
       Judaism
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Judaism">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Talmud" title="Talmud">
       Talmud
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1820" data-file-width="1257" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg/220px-Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg/330px-Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg/440px-Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Professir_Moshe_Halbertal.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Halbertal" title="Moshe Halbertal">
         Moshe Halbertal
        </a>
        in 2009.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Shlomo_ben_Aderet" title="Shlomo ben Aderet">
       Shlomo ben Aderet
      </a>
      criticized Christianity, adding that it has lesser form of monotheism, and lacks a unified deity compared to Judaism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/David_Flusser" title="David Flusser">
       David Flusser
      </a>
      viewed Christianity as "Cheaper Judaism" and highly anti-judaism, he also highlighted the "failure of christianity to convert the Jewish people to the new message" as "precisely the reason for the strong anti-jewish trend in christianity."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Professor
      <a href="/wiki/Moshe_Halbertal" title="Moshe Halbertal">
       Moshe Halbertal
      </a>
      , regards Christianity to be "idolatrous religion," and he further adds that the idolatry by Christians "opened the door to the easing of many other restrictive prohibitions."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Samuel_Wise" title="Stephen Samuel Wise">
       Stephen Samuel Wise
      </a>
      in his own words was critical towards Christian community, for their failure to rescue Jews, from Europe, during Nazi rule. He wrote that:-
     </p>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       A Christian world that will permit millions of Jews to be slain without moving heaven by prayer and earth in every human way to save its Jews has lost its capacity for moral and spiritual survival.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
        <a href="#cite_note-192">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         192
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Islam">
       Islam
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Islam">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Islam's prophet
      <a href="/wiki/Muhammad" title="Muhammad">
       Muhammad
      </a>
      said that Christians had to follow one God, but they have made multiple, he said:-
     </p>
     <blockquote class="toccolours" style="float:none; display:table;">
      <div style="padding: 10px 15px 10px 15px;">
       They have taken as lords beside Allah their rabbis and their monks and the Messiah son of Mary, when they were bidden to worship only One God.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
        <a href="#cite_note-193">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         193
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Muslim scholars have criticized Christianity, usually for its
      <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">
       Trinity
      </a>
      concept. They argue that this doctrine is an invention, distortion of the idea about God, and presentation of the idea that there are three gods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
       Origins
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Criticism_of_Christianity&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Origins">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Historicity_of_Jesus" title="Historicity of Jesus">
       Historicity of Jesus
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christ_myth_theory" title="Christ myth theory">
       Christ myth theory
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity_and_Paganism" title="Christianity and Paganism">
       Christianity and Paganism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Some have argued that Christianity is not founded on a historical Jesus, but rather on a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesus-Myth" title="Jesus-Myth">
       mythical creation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
       <a href="#cite_note-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This view proposes that the idea of Jesus was the Jewish manifestation of Hellenistic mystery cults that acknowledged the non-historic nature of their deity using it instead as a teaching device.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
       <a href="#cite_note-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Author Brian Branston has argued that Christianity adopted many mythological tales and traditions into its views of
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      . According to Branston these traditions, largely from Greco-Roman religions, have parallels to the story of Jesus.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
       <a href="#cite_note-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the position that Jesus was not a historical figure is essentially without support among
      <a href="/wiki/Biblical_studies" title="Biblical studies">
       biblical scholars
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_history" title="Classical history">
       classical historians
      </a>
      , most of whom regard its arguments as examples of
      <a href="/wiki/Pseudo-scholarship" title="Pseudo-scholarship">
       pseudo-scholarship
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
       <a href="#cite_note-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Scholars and historians such as
      <a href="/wiki/James_H._Charlesworth" title="James H. Charlesworth">
       James H. Charlesworth
      </a>
      caution against using parallels with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life-death-rebirth_deity" title="Life-death-rebirth deity">
       life-death-rebirth gods
      </a>
      in the widespread
      <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_mysteries" title="Greco-Roman mysteries">
       mystery religions
      </a>
      prevalent in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">
       Hellenistic
      </a>
      culture to conclude that Jesus is a purely legendary figure. Charlesworth argues that "it would be foolish to continue to foster the illusion that the Gospels are merely fictional stories like the legends of
      <a href="/wiki/Hercules" title="Hercules">
       Hercules
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Asclepius" title="Asclepius">
       Asclepius
      </a>
      . The theologies in the New Testament are grounded on interpretations of real historical events"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
       <a href="#cite_note-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>

       <li id="cite_note-15">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-15">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          W. H. Bellinger; William Reuben Farmer, eds. (1998).
          <a class="external text" href="http://books.google.com/books?id=EdfYAAAAMAAJ&amp;q=%22influence%22" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            Jesus and the Suffering Servant: Isaiah 53 and Christian Origins
           </i>
          </a>
          . Trinity Press.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781563382307" title="Special:BookSources/9781563382307">
           9781563382307
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            2 August
           </span>
           2013
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           Did Jesus of Nazareth live and die without the teaching about the righteous Servant of the Lord in Isaiah 53 having exerted any significant influence on his ministry? Is it probable that this text exerted no significant influence upon Jesus' understanding of the plan of God to save the nations that the prophet Isaiah sets forth?" Two questions addressed in a conference on "Isaiah 53 and Christian Origins" at Baylor University in the fall of 1995, the principal papers of which are available in "Jesus and the Suffering Servant.
          </q>

        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

          </a>
         </b>
        </span>

         <span class="citation web">

          <a class="external text" href="http://web.archive.org/web/20071026160950/http://www.ses.edu/journal/articles/2.1Hoffman.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           "A Jurisprudential Analysis Of Humes "in Principal" Argument Against Miracles"
          </a>
          <span style="font-size:85%;">
           (PDF)
          </span>
          . Christian Apologetics Journal, Volume 2, No. 1, Spring, 1999; Copyright 1999 by Southern Evangelical Seminary. Archived from
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.ses.edu/journal/articles/2.1Hoffman.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           the original
          </a>
          <span style="font-size:85%;">
           (PDF)
          </span>
          on October 26, 2007
          <span class="reference-accessdate">

           <span class="nowrap">

           </span>
          </span>

         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACriticism+of+Christianity&amp;rft.aulast=Paul+K.+Hoffman&amp;rft.au=Paul+K.+Hoffman&amp;rft.btitle=A+Jurisprudential+Analysis+Of+Hume%E2%80%99s+%22in+Principal%22+Argument+Against+Miracles&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ses.edu%2Fjournal%2Farticles%2F2.1Hoffman.pdf&amp;rft.pub=Christian+Apologetics+Journal%2C+Volume+2%2C+No.+1%2C+Spring%2C+1999%3B+Copyright+%C2%A91999+by+Southern+Evangelical+Seminary&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-49">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-49">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Melvin E. Page, Penny M. Sonnenburg (2003).
          <i>
           Colonialism: an international, social, cultural, and political encyclopedia, Volume 1
          </i>
          . ABC-CLIO. p.496.
          <q>
           Of all religions, Christianity has been most associated with colonialism because several of its forms (Catholicism and Protestantism) were the religions of the European powers engaged in colonial enterprise on a global scale.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACriticism+of+Christianity&amp;rft.aulast=Melvin+E.+Page%2C+Penny+M.+Sonnenburg&amp;rft.au=Melvin+E.+Page%2C+Penny+M.+Sonnenburg&amp;rft.btitle=Colonialism%3A+an+international%2C+social%2C+cultural%2C+and+political+encyclopedia%2C+Volume+1&amp;rft.date=2003&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=496&amp;rft.pub=ABC-CLIO&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-50">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-50">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          Bevans, Steven.
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.secondenlightenment.org/Christian%20Complicity.pdf" rel="nofollow">
           "Christian Complicity in Colonialism/ Globalism"
          </a>
          <span style="font-size:85%;">
           (PDF)
          </span>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            2010-11-17
           </span>
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           The modern missionary era was in many ways the religious arm of colonialism, whether Portuguese and Spanish colonialism in the sixteenth Century, or British, French, German, Belgian or American colonialism in the nineteenth. This was not all bad  oftentimes missionaries were heroic defenders of the rights of indigenous peoples
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACriticism+of+Christianity&amp;rft.au=Bevans%2C+Steven&amp;rft.aufirst=Steven&amp;rft.aulast=Bevans&amp;rft.btitle=Christian+Complicity+in+Colonialism%2F+Globalism&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.secondenlightenment.org%2FChristian%2520Complicity.pdf&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-51">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-51">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation journal">
          Andrews, Edward (2010).
          <a class="external text" href="http://jcs.oxfordjournals.org/content/early/2010/03/04/jcs.csp090" rel="nofollow">
           "Christian Missions and Colonial Empires Reconsidered: A Black Evangelist in West Africa, 17661816"
          </a>
          .
          <i>
           Journal of Church &amp; State
          </i>
          <b>
           51
          </b>
          (4): 663691.
          <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
           doi
          </a>
          :
          <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1093%2Fjcs%2Fcsp090" rel="nofollow">
           10.1093/jcs/csp090
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           Historians have traditionally looked at Christian missionaries in one of two ways. The first church historians to catalogue missionary history provided hagiographic descriptions of their trials, successes, and sometimes even martyrdom. Missionaries were thus visible saints, exemplars of ideal piety in a sea of persistent savagery. However, by the middle of the twentieth century, an era marked by civil rights movements, anti-colonialism, and growing secularization, missionaries were viewed quite differently. Instead of godly martyrs, historians now described missionaries as arrogant and rapacious imperialists. Christianity became not a saving grace but a monolithic and aggressive force that missionaries imposed upon defiant natives. Indeed, missionaries were now understood as important agents in the ever-expanding nation-state, or ideological shock troops for colonial invasion whose zealotry blinded them.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ACriticism+of+Christianity&amp;rft.atitle=Christian+Missions+and+Colonial+Empires+Reconsidered%3A+A+Black+Evangelist+in+West+Africa%2C+1766%E2%80%931816&amp;rft.au=Andrews%2C+Edward&amp;rft.aufirst=Edward&amp;rft.aulast=Andrews&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fjcs.oxfordjournals.org%2Fcontent%2Fearly%2F2010%2F03%2F04%2Fjcs.csp090&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1093%2Fjcs%2Fcsp090&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+Church+%26+State&amp;rft.pages=663-691&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.volume=51">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-52">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-52">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          Meador, Jake.
          <a class="external text" href="http://notesfromasmallplace.wordpress.com/2010/09/17/cosmetic-christianity-and-the-problem-of-colonialism-responding-to-brian-mclaren/" rel="nofollow">
           "Cosmetic Christianity and the Problem of Colonialism  Responding to Brian McLaren"
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            17 November
           </span>
           2010
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           According to Jake Meador, "some Christians have tried to make sense of post-colonial Christianity by renouncing practically everything about the Christianity of the colonizers. They reason that if the colonialists understanding of Christianity could be used to justify rape, murder, theft, and empire then their understanding of Christianity is completely wrong.
          </q>
         </span>

       <li id="cite_note-King-74">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

        <span class="reference-text">
         "King, Karen L. "
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/pages/frontline/shows/religion/first/women.html" rel="nofollow">
          Women in Ancient Christianity: the New Discoveries
         </a>
         ."
         <a href="/wiki/Karen_Leigh_King" title="Karen Leigh King">
          Karen L. King

       <li id="cite_note-Weaver-97">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Weaver_97-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Weaver_97-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          J. Denny Weaver (2001).
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.crosscurrents.org/weaver0701.htm#TEXT1" rel="nofollow">
           "Violence in Christian Theology"
          </a>
          . Cross Currents
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            28 October
           </span>
           2014
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           "[3rd paragraph] I am using broad definitions of the terms "violence" and "nonviolence." "Violence" means harm or damage, which obviously includes the direct violence of killing  in war, capital punishment, murder  but also covers the range of forms of systemic violence such as poverty, racism, and sexism. "Nonviolence" also covers a spectrum of attitudes and actions, from the classic Mennonite idea of passive nonresistance through active nonviolence and nonviolent resistance that would include various kinds of social action, confrontations and posing of alternatives that do not do bodily harm or injury.
          </q>
         </span>

        <span class="reference-text">
         "Let no cultured person draw near, none wise and none sensible, for all that kind of thing we count evil; but if any man is ignorant, if any man is wanting in sense and culture, if anybody is a fool, let him come boldly [to become a Christian].
         <a href="/wiki/Celsus" title="Celsus">

         </a>
         , AD178

       <li id="cite_note-114">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-114">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         "Since we all inherit Adam's sin, we all deserve eternal damnation. All who die unbaptized, even infants, will go to hell and suffer unending torment. We have no reason to complain of this, since we are all wicked. (In the Confessions, the Saint enumerates the crimes of which he was guilty in the cradle.) But by God's free grace certain people, among those who have been baptized, are chosen to go to heaven; these are the elect. They do not go to heaven because they are good; we are all totally depraved, except insofar as God's grace, which is only bestowed on the elect, enables us to be otherwise. No reason can be given why some are saved and the rest damned; this is due to God's unmotivated choice. Damnation proves God's justice; salvation His mercy. Both equally display His goodness."
         <i>
          A history of Western Philosophy by Bertrand Russell
         </i>
         , Simon &amp; Schuster, 1945
        </span>

        <span class="reference-text">

         <a href="/wiki/Michael_Grant_(author)" title="Michael Grant (author)">
          Michael Grant
         </a>
         stated, "To sum up, modern critical methods fail to support the Christ myth theory. It has 'again and again been answered and annihilated by first rank scholars.' In recent years, 'no serious scholar has ventured to postulate the non historicity of Jesus' or at any rate very few, and they have not succeeded in disposing of the much stronger, indeed very abundant, evidence to the contrary." Michael Grant,
         <i>
          Jesus: An Historian's Review of the Gospels
         </i>
         (Scribner, 1995).
        </span>
        <ul>
         <li>
          <span class="reference-text">

       </a>
       and atheist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sam_Harris_(author)" title="Sam Harris (author)">
        Sam Harris
       </a>
       as reported by
       <a href="/wiki/Newsweek" title="Newsweek">
        Newsweek
       </a>
       (April 9, 2007).
      </li>
      <li>
       "Does God Exist? The Nightline Face-Off." A video debate between Christians
       <a href="/wiki/Ray_Comfort" title="Ray Comfort">
        Ray Comfort
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Kirk_Cameron" title="Kirk Cameron">
        Kirk Cameron
       </a>
       and atheists Brian Sapient and Kelly O'Connor of the
       <a href="/wiki/Rational_Response_Squad" title="Rational Response Squad">
        Rational Response Squad
       </a>
       .
       <a class="external text" href="http://abcnews.go.com/Nightline/story?id=3148940&amp;page=1" rel="nofollow">
        Report
       </a>
       of the debate posted on the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nightline_(US_news_program)" title="Nightline (US news program)">
        Nightline
       </a>
       website.
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.wayofthemaster.com/abc_debate.shtml" rel="nofollow">
        Video
       </a>
       of the debate posted on
       <a href="/wiki/The_Way_of_the_Master" title="The Way of the Master">
        The Way of the Master
       </a>
       website.
      </li>
      <li>
       <a class="external text" href="http://www.infidels.org/library/modern/doug_jesseph/jesseph-craig/index.html" rel="nofollow">
        The Jesseph-Craig Debate: Does God Exist? (1996)
       </a>
       -Transcripts of a debate between Christian
       <a href="/wiki/William_Lane_Craig" title="William Lane Craig">
        William Lane Craig
       </a>
       and atheist Douglas M. Jesseph.
      </li>
     </ul>
